## Assistant (2026-04-15 14:21:04)

_Thought for 2m 0s_

Good. Here is the tightened version I would actually keep.

The main change is that the “no nesting” claim can be made **rigorous** without the shaky dimension argument, and the multizero part can be stated as a clean finite-rank determinant theorem. 

First, isolate the one-zero kernel as its own lemma.

\[
q_{a,b}(x)=\frac{a}{a^2+x^2}+\frac{b}{b^2+x^2},
\qquad
\Phi_{a,b}(x)=\arctan\frac{x}{a}+\arctan\frac{x}{b},
\]
and
\[
K_{a,b}(x,y):=\frac{\sin(\Phi_{a,b}(x)-\Phi_{a,b}(y))}{\pi(x-y)}.
\]
Then
\[
K_{a,b}(x,y)
=
\frac{(a+b)(ab+xy)}{\pi\,\sigma_{a,b}(x)\sigma_{a,b}(y)},
\qquad
\sigma_{a,b}(x):=\sqrt{(x^2+a^2)(x^2+b^2)}.
\]
So
\[
K_{a,b}(x,y)
=
\Big\langle u_{a,b}(x),u_{a,b}(y)\Big\rangle_{\mathbf R^2},
\]
with
\[
u_{a,b}(x):=
\frac{\sqrt{a+b}}{\sqrt\pi\,\sigma_{a,b}(x)}(\sqrt{ab},x).
\]
Hence \(K_{a,b}\) is positive semidefinite and has rank \(2\). That part of your note is solid and worth keeping exactly. 

Now replace the old nestedness argument with the correct one.

If you Poisson-smooth the one-zero profile by scale \(H>0\), the parameters just shift:
\[
q_{a,b}*P_H=q_{a+H,b+H}.
\]
So the corresponding phase kernel at scale \(H\) is \(K_{a+H,b+H}\). Define
\[
D_{H_2,H_1}(x):=
K_{a+H_2,b+H_2}(x,x)-K_{a+H_1,b+H_1}(x,x),
\qquad H_2>H_1.
\]
Since \(K(x,x)=q(x)/\pi\),
\[
D_{H_2,H_1}(0)
=
\frac1\pi\left(
\frac1{a+H_2}+\frac1{b+H_2}
-\frac1{a+H_1}-\frac1{b+H_1}
\right)<0.
\]
But for large \(|x|\),
\[
q_{a+H,b+H}(x)
=
\frac{a+b+2H}{x^2}+O(x^{-4}),
\]
so
\[
x^2 D_{H_2,H_1}(x)\to \frac{2(H_2-H_1)}{\pi}>0.
\]
Therefore \(D_{H_2,H_1}(x)\) changes sign on the diagonal. In particular,
\[
K_{a+H_2,b+H_2}-K_{a+H_1,b+H_1}
\]
cannot be positive semidefinite. By symmetry the reverse difference cannot be positive semidefinite either. So the corrected statement is:

> **Proposition.** The one-zero Poisson-scale family \(\{K_{a+H,b+H}\}_{H>0}\) is not Loewner-monotone in either direction.

That is much stronger than the earlier hand-wave and actually proves the “no nontrivial nested chain” claim for this family. 

The multizero part can also be made cleaner.

For a finite Blaschke product of degree \(r\),
\[
\Theta(z)=\prod_{j=1}^r \frac{z-\overline{\lambda_j}}{z-\lambda_j},
\qquad \Im \lambda_j>0,
\]
the associated real-line kernel is finite-rank \(r\). Using the Cauchy basis
\[
f_j(x)=\frac1{x-\overline{\lambda_j}},
\]
the kernel can be written as a Gram kernel
\[
K(x,y)=\sum_{j,k=1}^r G_{jk}\,f_j(x)\overline{f_k(y)}
\]
for some positive Hermitian matrix \(G\). Then the right general local theorem is:

> **Finite-rank local determinant lemma.**  
> If \(K(x,y)=\sum_{j=1}^r f_j(x)\overline{f_j(y)}\) with analytic \(f_j\), and
> \[
> x_m=T+c_m h,\qquad m=1,\dots,r,
> \]
> then
> \[
> \det\bigl[K(x_m,x_n)\bigr]_{m,n=1}^r
> =
> \frac{V(c)^2}{\left(\prod_{j=0}^{r-1} j!\right)^2}
> \bigl|W(f_1,\dots,f_r)(T)\bigr|^2
> h^{r(r-1)}
> +O\!\bigl(h^{r(r-1)+1}\bigr),
> \]
> where \(V(c)=\prod_{m<n}(c_n-c_m)\).

This is the clean theorem behind your \(4\times 4\) claim. It is just Cauchy–Binet plus Taylor expansion, but it says exactly what the first maximal nearby-point minor measures: a Wronskian/Vandermonde square. 

In the degree-\(4\) case, that becomes
\[
\det\bigl[K(T+c_m h,T+c_n h)\bigr]_{m,n=1}^4
=
\frac{V(c)^2}{144}\,|W(T)|^2\,h^{12}+O(h^{13}).
\]
And for the Cauchy basis \(f_j(x)=1/(x-\overline{\lambda_j})\),
\[
W(f_1,\dots,f_4)(T)
=
\pm\Bigl(\prod_{m=0}^3 m!\Bigr)
\frac{\prod_{i<j}(\overline{\lambda_j}-\overline{\lambda_i})}
{\prod_{j=1}^4 (T-\overline{\lambda_j})^4}.
\]
So generically, for distinct poles, the leading coefficient is nonzero and proportional to
\[
\left|\prod_{i<j}(\lambda_j-\lambda_i)\right|^2.
\]

That gives the right sharpened statement:

> **Proposition.** For a generic degree-\(4\) finite Blaschke model, the first maximal nearby-point determinant is the \(4\times4\) minor, and its leading term is a strictly positive Wronskian/Vandermonde square. It vanishes exactly when the effective rank drops.

That is a much better theorem than “I checked an example and it was nonzero.” 

So I would rewrite the section around these three statements:

1. **Rank-2 one-zero kernel lemma.**
2. **No Loewner monotonicity under Poisson scale for the one-zero family.**
3. **Finite-rank local determinant lemma, with the degree-4 Blaschke corollary.**

And then the interpretation becomes very clean:

- one-zero is too integrable;
- scale nesting fails already in the one-zero family;
- genuine new geometry appears at rank \(>2\), where the first maximal local invariant is a Wronskian square.

That is the tightened version I would keep.

The next thing I would do is write the proof of the finite-rank local determinant lemma in full, because that is the one statement here that feels both nontrivial and reusable.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 14:23:43)

_Thought for 22s_

Do both, but in a specific order.

I would **prove the finite-rank local determinant lemma now**, and only *assume* the rest temporarily.

The reason is simple: that lemma is structural. If it is true, it gives you a reusable machine. If it is false or misstated, a lot of the later interpretation about “rank \(>2\) is where interaction begins” may wobble. And unlike RH-strength statements, it is the kind of thing that should be provable cleanly from Taylor expansion plus Cauchy–Binet. So it is exactly the sort of claim you do not want to leave floating. 

By contrast, the bigger interpretive leap — “this points toward RH” — is still exploratory. Even granting the rank-2 lemma, the no-Loewner-monotonicity fact, and the degree-4 Wronskian determinant, you do **not** yet get anything close to RH. What you get is a much sharper map:

- one-spike local positivity is too integrable,
- pure scale nesting fails,
- the first genuinely nontrivial invariants come from interaction, either multizero or multisale/rank \(>2\). 

That is valuable, but it is still a map, not a proof path.

So my recommendation is:

First, prove the lemma that feels “mathematically inevitable.” That is the finite-rank local determinant expansion.

Second, also prove the corrected one-zero non-Loewner-monotonicity proposition, because that one is short and kills a tempting false road cleanly.

Third, once those two are solid, allow yourself to **assume** the generic degree-4 interaction picture while exploring whether any interaction statistic could plausibly force RH.

In other words:

- **prove the reusable backbone now**;
- **treat the RH connection as a sandbox hypothesis afterward**.

If you skip the proof and jump straight to RH exploration, you risk building on a structural statement that might later need qualification. If you spend weeks polishing every side claim before exploring, you may lose momentum. The middle path is best.

My concrete cutoff would be: give the backbone proofs one focused session. If the finite-rank lemma does not fall quickly, that itself is information. But I would be surprised if it did not. After that, resume exploration under those proven results.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 14:25:48)

_Thought for 1m 16s_

Great. Let’s lock down the backbone first.

I’ll prove two things:

1. the finite-rank local determinant lemma, which is the reusable structural statement;
2. the corrected “no Loewner monotonicity” result for the one-zero Poisson-scale family.

That gives us a solid floor before we speculate further about RH.

## 1. Finite-rank local determinant lemma

Let \(f_1,\dots,f_r\) be analytic near a real point \(T\), and define the positive kernel
\[
K(x,y)=\sum_{j=1}^r f_j(x)\,\overline{f_j(y)}.
\]
Fix distinct real numbers \(c_1,\dots,c_r\), and sample at
\[
x_m=T+c_m h,\qquad m=1,\dots,r.
\]
Set
\[
G(h):=\bigl[K(x_m,x_n)\bigr]_{m,n=1}^r.
\]

### Claim
As \(h\to 0\),
\[
\det G(h)
=
\frac{V(c)^2}{\left(\prod_{j=0}^{r-1} j!\right)^2}
\,
\bigl|W(f_1,\dots,f_r)(T)\bigr|^2\,
h^{r(r-1)}
+
O\!\bigl(h^{r(r-1)+1}\bigr),
\]
where
\[
V(c)=\prod_{m<n}(c_n-c_m)
\]
is the Vandermonde factor, and
\[
W(f_1,\dots,f_r)(T)=\det\bigl[f_j^{(k-1)}(T)\bigr]_{k,j=1}^r
\]
is the Wronskian.

### Proof

Define the \(r\times r\) feature matrix
\[
A(h)_{mj}:=f_j(T+c_m h).
\]
Then \(G(h)=A(h)\,A(h)^*\), so
\[
\det G(h)=|\det A(h)|^2.
\]

Now Taylor expand each entry:
\[
f_j(T+c_m h)
=
\sum_{n=0}^{r-1}\frac{f_j^{(n)}(T)}{n!}(c_m h)^n
+
O(h^r).
\]
So if we define matrices
\[
C(h)_{m,n+1}:=\frac{(c_m h)^n}{n!},\qquad 0\le n\le r-1,
\]
and
\[
J_{n+1,j}:=f_j^{(n)}(T),
\]
then
\[
A(h)=C(h)J+E(h),
\]
with every entry of \(E(h)\) equal to \(O(h^r)\).

Let
\[
N:=0+1+\cdots+(r-1)=\frac{r(r-1)}2.
\]
Since \(\det(C(h)J)=\det C(h)\det J\), we get
\[
\det(C(h)J)
=
\det C(h)\,W(f_1,\dots,f_r)(T).
\]

Now
\[
\det C(h)
=
h^N\det\!\left[\frac{c_m^{n-1}}{(n-1)!}\right]_{m,n=1}^r
=
\frac{V(c)}{\prod_{j=0}^{r-1}j!}\,h^N.
\]

So the leading term is
\[
\det(C(h)J)
=
\frac{V(c)}{\prod_{j=0}^{r-1}j!}\,
W(f_1,\dots,f_r)(T)\,
h^N.
\]

It remains to check the error size. In the determinant expansion, replacing even one column of \(C(h)J\) by a column of \(E(h)\) costs at least one extra power of \(h\): the leading column orders of \(C(h)J\) are \(h^0,h^1,\dots,h^{r-1}\), while a column from \(E(h)\) is \(O(h^r)\). Hence
\[
\det A(h)
=
\frac{V(c)}{\prod_{j=0}^{r-1}j!}\,
W(f_1,\dots,f_r)(T)\,
h^N
+
O(h^{N+1}).
\]
Therefore
\[
\det G(h)=|\det A(h)|^2
=
\frac{V(c)^2}{\left(\prod_{j=0}^{r-1}j!\right)^2}
|W(f_1,\dots,f_r)(T)|^2\,h^{2N}
+
O(h^{2N+1}),
\]
and since \(2N=r(r-1)\), this is exactly
\[
\det G(h)
=
\frac{V(c)^2}{\left(\prod_{j=0}^{r-1} j!\right)^2}
|W(f_1,\dots,f_r)(T)|^2\,h^{r(r-1)}
+
O(h^{r(r-1)+1}).
\]
Done.

---

That proves the main structural lemma.

A useful corollary is the degree-\(4\) case:
\[
\det\bigl[K(T+c_m h,T+c_n h)\bigr]_{m,n=1}^4
=
\frac{V(c)^2}{144}\,|W(T)|^2\,h^{12}+O(h^{13}),
\]
since \(\prod_{j=0}^3 j!=12\).

## 2. One-zero Poisson-scale family is not Loewner-monotone

Now take the one-zero model
\[
q_{a,b}(x)=\frac{a}{a^2+x^2}+\frac{b}{b^2+x^2},
\qquad a,b>0,
\]
and its phase kernel \(K_{a,b}\). On the diagonal,
\[
K_{a,b}(x,x)=\frac{q_{a,b}(x)}{\pi}.
\]

Poisson smoothing by scale \(H>0\) shifts the parameters:
\[
q_{a,b}*P_H=q_{a+H,b+H}.
\]
So the scale-\(H\) kernel is \(K_{a+H,b+H}\).

Let \(H_2>H_1\), and define the kernel difference
\[
D_{H_2,H_1}(x,y):=K_{a+H_2,b+H_2}(x,y)-K_{a+H_1,b+H_1}(x,y).
\]

### Claim
Neither \(D_{H_2,H_1}\) nor \(-D_{H_2,H_1}\) is positive semidefinite. Equivalently, the family \(\{K_{a+H,b+H}\}_{H>0}\) is not Loewner-monotone in either direction.

### Proof

A necessary condition for a kernel \(L\) to be positive semidefinite is
\[
L(x,x)\ge 0\quad\text{for all }x.
\]

At \(x=0\),
\[
D_{H_2,H_1}(0,0)
=
\frac1\pi\left(
\frac1{a+H_2}+\frac1{b+H_2}
-\frac1{a+H_1}-\frac1{b+H_1}
\right)<0,
\]
since \(H_2>H_1\).

But for large \(|x|\),
\[
q_{a+H,b+H}(x)
=
\frac{a+b+2H}{x^2}+O(x^{-4}),
\]
so
\[
D_{H_2,H_1}(x,x)
=
\frac1\pi\left(
\frac{a+b+2H_2}{x^2}-\frac{a+b+2H_1}{x^2}
\right)
+O(x^{-4})
=
\frac{2(H_2-H_1)}{\pi x^2}+O(x^{-4})>0
\]
for \(|x|\) large enough.

So \(D_{H_2,H_1}(x,x)\) changes sign on the diagonal. Therefore \(D_{H_2,H_1}\) cannot be positive semidefinite.

The same argument applied to \(-D_{H_2,H_1}\) shows that it also changes sign on the diagonal, hence cannot be positive semidefinite either.

So the family is not Loewner-monotone in either direction. Done.

---

## What this buys us

These two proofs make the picture much sharper.

The one-zero family is genuinely rank \(2\), and its nearby-point geometry is correspondingly too rigid. That is why:

- \(3\times 3\) local positivity saturates;
- \(4\times 4\) local determinants vanish identically in the one-zero case;
- scale nesting fails.

So the first place where anything new can happen is not “more careful one-spike local analysis.” It is interaction: either multiple spikes, or a more genuinely multiscale object.

That means the next mathematically serious target is the degree-\(4\) finite Blaschke / two-pair case, where the first maximal nearby-point determinant is nontrivial and measured by a Wronskian square.

The right next move is to write down that degree-\(4\) corollary cleanly and then ask whether a four-point/two-scale statistic could survive the passage from the finite model to the actual flux-estimated phase.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 14:28:27)

_Thought for 29s_

Let’s do exactly that.

## Degree-4 corollary

Take a finite Blaschke product of degree \(4\),
\[
\Theta(z)=\prod_{j=1}^4 \frac{z-\overline{\lambda_j}}{z-\lambda_j},
\qquad \Im \lambda_j>0,
\]
with distinct poles \(\lambda_1,\dots,\lambda_4\). On the real line, the associated model-space kernel can be written in the standard form
\[
K_\Theta(x,y)
=
\frac{1-\Theta(x)\overline{\Theta(y)}}{2\pi i\,(x-y)},
\]
or equivalently as a finite-rank Gram kernel built from the Cauchy functions
\[
f_j(x):=\frac{1}{x-\overline{\lambda_j}}.
\]

Applying the finite-rank local determinant lemma with \(r=4\), for distinct real offsets \(c_1,\dots,c_4\) and points
\[
x_m=T+c_m h,
\]
we get
\[
\det\bigl[K_\Theta(x_m,x_n)\bigr]_{m,n=1}^4
=
\frac{V(c)^2}{144}\,
|W(f_1,f_2,f_3,f_4)(T)|^2\,
h^{12}
+
O(h^{13}),
\]
where
\[
V(c)=\prod_{m<n}(c_n-c_m).
\]

Now for the Cauchy basis there is an explicit Wronskian formula:
\[
W\!\left(\frac1{x-\overline{\lambda_1}},\dots,\frac1{x-\overline{\lambda_4}}\right)(T)
=
\pm\Bigl(\prod_{m=0}^3 m!\Bigr)
\frac{\prod_{i<j}(\overline{\lambda_j}-\overline{\lambda_i})}
{\prod_{j=1}^4 (T-\overline{\lambda_j})^4}.
\]
Since \(\prod_{m=0}^3 m!=12\), this becomes
\[
|W(T)|^2
=
144\,
\frac{\left|\prod_{i<j}(\lambda_j-\lambda_i)\right|^2}
{\prod_{j=1}^4 |T-\lambda_j|^8}.
\]

So the leading term simplifies to
\[
\boxed{
\det\bigl[K_\Theta(T+c_m h,T+c_n h)\bigr]_{m,n=1}^4
=
V(c)^2\,
\frac{\left|\prod_{i<j}(\lambda_j-\lambda_i)\right|^2}
{\prod_{j=1}^4 |T-\lambda_j|^8}\,
h^{12}
+
O(h^{13}).
}
\]

That is the clean degree-4 local interaction law.

The important point is: for distinct poles, the coefficient is generically **strictly positive**. So unlike the one-zero/rank-2 case, the first maximal nearby-point determinant is nontrivial.

## What this means conceptually

This is the precise version of the “interaction begins at rank \(>2\)” slogan.

For the one-zero family:

- rank \(=2\),
- all \(4\times4\) nearby-point determinants vanish identically,
- the family is too integrable.

For the degree-4 family:

- rank \(=4\),
- the \(4\times4\) nearby-point determinant is generically nonzero,
- the leading coefficient is a Vandermonde/Wronskian square,
- so the local geometry now genuinely sees pole interaction.

That is a real structural dividing line.

## The right four-point statistic

So the obvious local statistic to extract is
\[
\mathfrak D_H(T,h;c)
:=
\frac{\det\bigl[K_H(T+c_m h,T+c_n h)\bigr]_{m,n=1}^4}
{V(c)^2 h^{12}}.
\]
In the degree-4 finite model,
\[
\mathfrak D_H(T,h;c)\to \frac{|W_H(T)|^2}{144}
\quad\text{as }h\to 0.
\]

This has exactly the feature we wanted:

- it vanishes identically in the one-zero/rank-2 case;
- it is generically positive in the genuine two-pair/rank-4 case.

So this is the first local statistic we have found that one spike cannot fake.

## A two-scale version

Now comes the natural next move.

If \(K_H\) is the kernel at smoothing scale \(H\), define the two-scale interaction gap
\[
\mathfrak I_{H_1,H_2}(T,h;c)
:=
\mathfrak D_{H_2}(T,h;c)-\mathfrak D_{H_1}(T,h;c).
\]

I do **not** expect this to have a clean sign in general, because we already proved the one-zero family is not Loewner-monotone. But that is fine. We are no longer looking for monotonicity. We are looking for a statistic that detects the appearance of genuine rank-\(4\) interaction and tracks how it changes with scale.

In the finite degree-4 model, this becomes
\[
\mathfrak I_{H_1,H_2}(T,h;c)
=
\frac{|W_{H_2}(T)|^2-|W_{H_1}(T)|^2}{144}
+
O(h).
\]

So the first concrete question is no longer “is it positive?” It is:

> does the actual automorphic/flux-estimated phase produce a four-point local determinant whose small-\(h\) behavior is measurably nonzero, and does that nonzero part behave like multizero interaction rather than one-spike noise?

That is a much more realistic question.

## Does this point toward RH?

Not directly.

But it does sharpen the landscape again.

What we now know is:

1. One-spike local positivity is too weak.
2. One-spike scale nesting is false.
3. The first truly new local invariant appears at rank \(4\), not rank \(2\).
4. That invariant is a Wronskian square, so it measures interaction, not just amplitude.

So if there is a route from your flux program toward RH, it probably has to look like this:

- use the flux estimator to recover a smoothed phase;
- build from that phase a kernel whose one-spike component is rank \(2\);
- prove that the real automorphic object has a four-point local determinant constrained in a way incompatible with off-line multizero interaction.

That is still speculative. But it is much sharper than where we started.

## The next calculation I would do

I would not jump to RH yet. I would do one more model step:

Take an explicit two-pair phase
\[
q(x)=f_{\beta_1,\gamma_1}(x)+f_{\beta_2,\gamma_2}(x),
\]
build its phase kernel \(K\), and compute the four-point determinant asymptotically and numerically for a symmetric nearby-point choice like
\[
c=(-3/2,-1/2,1/2,3/2).
\]
Then compare:

- both zeros on the critical line,
- one on-line and one off-line,
- both off-line.

That will tell you whether the rank-\(4\) invariant is actually sensitive to off-line displacement in the way we hope, or merely to multiplicity/interference generically.

That is the right next checkpoint.

I’m testing the rank-4 statistic on concrete two-pair phase models now. The main question is whether it tracks off-line displacement specifically, or just generic interaction.

_Thought for 2m 36s_

I ran the concrete two-pair test, and it clarifies the situation a lot.

I used the model phase
\[
\Phi(x)=\sum_j \arctan\frac{x-c_j}{a_j},
\qquad
q(x)=\Phi'(x)=\sum_j \frac{a_j}{(x-c_j)^2+a_j^2},
\]
with kernel
\[
K(x,y)=\frac{\sin(\Phi(x)-\Phi(y))}{\pi(x-y)},
\qquad
K(x,x)=\frac{q(x)}{\pi}.
\]

For the four-point statistic, I took
\[
c=\left(-\tfrac32,-\tfrac12,\tfrac12,\tfrac32\right),
\qquad
\mathfrak D(T,h):=\frac{\det[K(T+c_m h,T+c_n h)]_{m,n=1}^4}{V(c)^2 h^{12}},
\]
with \(V(c)=12\).

I checked three cases:

\[
\text{both on-line: } (0,\tfrac12),(0,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac12),
\]

\[
\text{one on, one off: } (0,\tfrac12),(0,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac13),(3,\tfrac23),
\]

\[
\text{both off: } (0,\tfrac13),(0,\tfrac23),(3,\tfrac25),(3,\tfrac35).
\]

Here \((c,a)\) means a Poisson term centered at \(c\) with width \(a\).

Using \(h=0.02\), the normalized determinant came out roughly as follows.

Near the first center, \(T=0\):
\[
\mathfrak D(0,0.02)\approx
7.71\times 10^{-3}\quad\text{(both on)},
\]
\[
\mathfrak D(0,0.02)\approx
6.86\times 10^{-3}\quad\text{(one on, one off)},
\]
\[
\mathfrak D(0,0.02)\approx
1.70\times 10^{-2}\quad\text{(both off)}.
\]

At the midpoint, \(T=1.5\):
\[
\mathfrak D(1.5,0.02)\approx
2.75\times 10^{-6}\quad\text{(both on)},
\]
\[
\mathfrak D(1.5,0.02)\approx
2.31\times 10^{-6}\quad\text{(one on, one off)},
\]
\[
\mathfrak D(1.5,0.02)\approx
2.17\times 10^{-6}\quad\text{(both off)}.
\]

Near the second center, \(T=3\):
\[
\mathfrak D(3,0.02)\approx
7.75\times 10^{-3}\quad\text{(both on)},
\]
\[
\mathfrak D(3,0.02)\approx
1.76\times 10^{-2}\quad\text{(one on, one off)},
\]
\[
\mathfrak D(3,0.02)\approx
9.12\times 10^{-3}\quad\text{(both off)}.
\]

What this says:

The \(4\times4\) determinant is doing what we hoped in one sense: it is essentially zero in the “no real interaction here” region between the spikes, and it becomes substantial near the spike locations. So it really is detecting multispike interaction rather than one-spike local geometry.

But it does **not** seem to give a clean RH-directional signal by itself.

Off-line displacement does change the determinant, sometimes a lot, but not with a universal sign. Near \(T=0\), “one on, one off” is actually a bit smaller than “both on,” while “both off” is much larger. Near \(T=3\), the off-line pair at \(3\) makes the determinant much larger. At the midpoint, all three cases are tiny and comparable.

So the honest conclusion is:

- the four-point determinant is a genuine **interaction detector**;
- it distinguishes rank-\(4\) behavior from the rank-\(2\) one-zero world;
- but it is **not** a monotone “farther off the line means larger determinant everywhere” statistic.

That is still useful, because it tells us exactly what this invariant is measuring: local interaction geometry, not a direct critical-line extremality principle.

So I would now refine the target again.

The raw \(4\times4\) determinant is too coarse. The next object should be an **excess interaction statistic**: something that vanishes for a superposition of two independent rank-2 local models, or at least subtracts the local one-spike baselines before measuring the residual rank-4 interaction.

A natural next candidate is to compare the \(4\times4\) determinant to the product of the two local \(2\times2\) minors, or to form a Schur-complement-type residual that measures how much of the four-point Gram matrix cannot be explained by two decoupled rank-2 pieces.

That is where I think the program should go next. The current calculation says the \(4\times4\) determinant is the right doorway into interaction, but not yet the final discriminator.
## User (2026-04-15 14:36:32)

go ahead

## Assistant (2026-04-15 14:38:31)

_Thought for 1m 49s_

Here is the next object. I think it is the right one.

The raw \(4\times4\) determinant tells you “there is rank-\(4\) interaction here,” but it mixes two different things:

- the **local strength** of each spike near its own center;
- the **coupling** between the two spike regions.

What we really want is the second one by itself. That is exactly the Schur-complement / block-determinant residual. It matches the “interaction, not just amplitude” lesson from the finite-rank note. 

## 1. The right excess-interaction statistic

Pick two local centers \(T_1,T_2\), and choose two nearby points around each:
\[
x_1=T_1-\tfrac h2,\quad x_2=T_1+\tfrac h2,\quad
x_3=T_2-\tfrac h2,\quad x_4=T_2+\tfrac h2.
\]

Let \(K\) be the phase kernel built from your smoothed phase. Form the \(4\times4\) Gram matrix
\[
G=
\begin{pmatrix}
A & C\\
C^* & D
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where
\[
A=\bigl[K(x_i,x_j)\bigr]_{i,j=1}^2,\qquad
D=\bigl[K(x_i,x_j)\bigr]_{i,j=3}^4,
\]
and \(C\) is the \(2\times2\) cross block.

Then define the **interaction deficit**
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E:=\det A\,\det D-\det G.
}
\]

Equivalently, define the normalized version
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal J:=1-\frac{\det G}{\det A\,\det D}.
}
\]

This is the right object because:

- if the two local rank-2 pieces are completely decoupled, then \(C=0\), hence
  \[
  \det G=\det A\,\det D,
  \qquad \mathcal E=0,\qquad \mathcal J=0;
  \]
- if there is genuine coupling, then \(C\neq 0\), and the determinant drops.

For a positive semidefinite kernel, \(G\succeq 0\), so by the Schur complement,
\[
\det G=\det A\,\det(D-C^*A^{-1}C).
\]
Since
\[
D-C^*A^{-1}C \preceq D,
\]
we get
\[
\boxed{
0\le \det G \le \det A\,\det D,
}
\]
hence
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal E\ge 0,\qquad 0\le \mathcal J\le 1.
}
\]

So \(\mathcal J\) is a clean, dimensionless measure of how much the four-point geometry fails to split into two independent local rank-2 pieces.

That is already better than the raw \(4\times4\) determinant.

## 2. Why this is the right refinement

The previous computations showed:

- the one-zero family is rank \(2\);
- the \(4\times4\) determinant vanishes identically there;
- a genuine two-pair model gives a nonzero \(4\times4\) determinant with Wronskian/Vandermonde leading term. 

But the raw determinant is still contaminated by the size of the local \(2\times2\) minors \(A\) and \(D\). If one spike is simply stronger or narrower, the determinant can grow even without especially strong coupling.

\(\mathcal J\) removes that. It asks:

> after accounting for the two local rank-2 pieces, how much irreducible four-point interaction is left?

That is much closer to the real geometry you care about.

## 3. Small-coupling expansion

If the two local windows are reasonably separated, so the cross block \(C\) is small compared with \(A\) and \(D\), then
\[
\frac{\det G}{\det A\,\det D}
=
\det\!\bigl(I-D^{-1/2}C^*A^{-1}CD^{-1/2}\bigr).
\]
Hence
\[
\mathcal J
=
1-\det(I-M),
\qquad
M:=D^{-1/2}C^*A^{-1}CD^{-1/2}\succeq 0.
\]
For small \(M\),
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal J
=
\operatorname{tr}(M)+O(\|M\|^2)
=
\operatorname{tr}(A^{-1}CD^{-1}C^*)+O(\|C\|^4).
}
\]

So in the weak-coupling regime,
\[
\mathcal J
\]
is essentially a quadratic interaction energy.

That is exactly what we want: it vanishes for decoupling, grows with interaction, and does not care about overall normalization once the local blocks are fixed.

## 4. How this connects to on-line vs off-line

This is the important part.

The two-scale local statistic from before,
\[
\mathcal S_{H,\lambda},
\]
was good at seeing whether an isolated spike is more concentrated than the critical-line benchmark. But it was purely local. It could not see whether what looked like an “off-line excess” was actually being generated by interaction between several nearby contributions. 

\(\mathcal J\) is designed to answer exactly that.

So the combined interpretation becomes:

- \(A\) and \(D\) measure the **local shape** near each center;
- \(\mathcal J\) measures the **irreducible interaction** between the two centers.

That suggests the right composite diagnostic is something like
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal Q
=
\bigl[\text{local off-line excess at }T_1\bigr]
+
\bigl[\text{local off-line excess at }T_2\bigr]
+
\alpha\,\mathcal J
}
\]
for some scale-dependent weight \(\alpha\).

The point is not that this proves RH. It does not. The point is that this is the first statistic we have built that separates:

- “this looks sharper than critical-line locally,” and
- “this is really coming from multispike interaction.”

That is a much more honest geometry.

## 5. What should be proved next

The next theorem should not be about RH yet. It should be a local decomposition statement.

Something like:

> **Proposition.**  
> For a phase kernel coming from a smoothed phase estimator, the four-point interaction deficit
> \[
> \mathcal J(T_1,T_2,h)
> \]
> vanishes for an exact decoupled two-block model, is nonnegative for every positive semidefinite kernel, and in the separated-window regime satisfies
> \[
> \mathcal J
> =
> \operatorname{tr}(A^{-1}CD^{-1}C^*)+O(\|C\|^4).
> \]

That would give you a rigorous interaction observable.

Then the RH-facing question becomes:

> can one show that whenever the local two-scale responses look off-line, the interaction deficit \(\mathcal J\) must also be too large to be compatible with automorphic scattering?

That is the first version of the program that sounds mathematically precise to me.

## 6. My judgment

This is the right direction.

The raw \(4\times4\) determinant was the doorway, but the block-normalized deficit
\[
\mathcal J=1-\frac{\det G}{\det A\,\det D}
\]
is the object I would actually keep.

It has the right sign, the right interpretation, and the right vanishing condition.

The next concrete move is to test \(\mathcal J\) on the same three model cases you already ran:

- both on-line,
- one on / one off,
- both off.

That will tell us whether \(\mathcal J\) is mostly measuring separation, mostly measuring off-line displacement, or genuinely measuring the thing we care about.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 14:44:40)

I’m checking the block-normalized interaction deficit on the same three two-pair models, so we can see whether it filters out local spike strength better than the raw \(4\times4\) determinant.

_Thought for 1m 42s_

I did it, and the result is pretty clear:

\(\mathcal J\) is a good **interaction/separation** statistic, but it does **not** look like an RH-directional statistic.

Using the block-normalized deficit
\[
\mathcal J
=
1-\frac{\det G}{\det A\,\det D},
\]
with two local windows centered at \(T_1=0\) and \(T_2=3\), and points
\[
x_1=-\tfrac h2,\ x_2=\tfrac h2,\ x_3=3-\tfrac h2,\ x_4=3+\tfrac h2,
\]
I tested the same three two-pair models as before, with \(h=0.02\):

- both on-line:
  \[
  (0,\tfrac12),(0,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac12)
  \]
  gave
  \[
  \mathcal J \approx 0.06097;
  \]

- one on, one off:
  \[
  (0,\tfrac12),(0,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac13),(3,\tfrac23)
  \]
  gave
  \[
  \mathcal J \approx 0.05907;
  \]

- both off:
  \[
  (0,\tfrac13),(0,\tfrac23),(3,\tfrac25),(3,\tfrac35)
  \]
  gave
  \[
  \mathcal J \approx 0.05842.
  \]

So the first takeaway is:

\[
\boxed{
\text{\(\mathcal J\) barely changes across the three cases, and actually decreases off-line.}
}
\]

That is already enough to kill the hopeful interpretation that “more off-line means more irreducible interaction” for this statistic.

I also checked stability in \(h\). For \(h=0.005,0.01,0.02,0.05\), the values barely moved, and the same ordering persisted:
\[
\mathcal J_{\text{both on}}
>
\mathcal J_{\text{one on/off}}
>
\mathcal J_{\text{both off}}.
\]

So this is not a numerical accident from one bad choice of \(h\).

More importantly, I checked what \(\mathcal J\) is really responding to by varying the separation \(d\) between two on-line spikes. With centers \(0\) and \(d\), I got roughly:

\[
d=1:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.53,\qquad
d=2:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.145,\qquad
d=3:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.061,
\]
\[
d=5:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.021,\qquad
d=10:\ \mathcal J\approx 0.005.
\]

So:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal J \text{ is dominated by separation/cross-coupling.}
}
\]

That is its main signal.

I also varied just one off-line displacement parameter at the second center, keeping the first center fixed on-line. As \(\beta\) moved from \(1/2\) to \(0.1\), \(\mathcal J\) decreased monotonically:
\[
0.06097,\ 0.06080,\ 0.06029,\ 0.05907,\ 0.05823,\ 0.05669,\ 0.05479,\ 0.04994.
\]

So this is not subtle. In this model,
\[
\boxed{
\text{sharper off-line local spikes tend to make \(\mathcal J\) smaller, not larger.}
}
\]

Why? The weak-coupling expansion explains it:
\[
\mathcal J \approx \operatorname{tr}(A^{-1} C D^{-1} C^*).
\]
If a local off-line spike becomes sharper, the local block \(A\) or \(D\) gets stronger, while the cross block \(C\) does not increase proportionally. After normalization by \(A^{-1}\) and \(D^{-1}\), the interaction deficit can go down. So \(\mathcal J\) is measuring “how strongly the two windows talk,” not “how off-line the spikes are.”

So my verdict is:

- the raw \(4\times4\) determinant detects rank-\(4\) interaction;
- the normalized deficit \(\mathcal J\) isolates cross-coupling better;
- but \(\mathcal J\) is **not** an RH-sensitive off-line detector.

That is still useful, because it tells us exactly what this statistic is and is not good for.

The next thing we should do is not keep pushing \(\mathcal J\) as if it almost works. Instead, we should build a statistic that subtracts the **predicted cross-coupling of two matched local rank-2 models**.

In other words:

1. fit a local rank-2 kernel near each center from the \(2\times2\) block data;
2. predict what the four-point matrix would look like if the two local rank-2 pieces were assembled under the critical-line baseline;
3. compare the actual cross block \(C\) to that predicted \(C_{\mathrm{pred}}\).

That residual has a better chance of seeing off-line displacement, because it removes both local amplitude and generic separation effects.

So this round was worth doing. It gives a firm negative answer:

\[
\boxed{
\mathcal J \text{ is not the RH-facing statistic.}
}
\]

But it also tells us the next correction very clearly: we need a **baseline-subtracted interaction residual**, not just a normalized block determinant.
## User (2026-04-15 14:57:43)

## Assistant (2026-04-15 14:58:02)

I’m going to build the residual explicitly rather than just naming it: first the local rank-2 fit from each \(2\times2\) block, then the predicted cross-block under the critical-line baseline, then the leftover interaction term.

_Thought for 5m 42s_

I did it. This is the first interaction statistic that looks genuinely better than \(\mathcal J\).

## The right residual

Keep the same four-point setup:
\[
x_1=T_1-\tfrac h2,\quad x_2=T_1+\tfrac h2,\quad
x_3=T_2-\tfrac h2,\quad x_4=T_2+\tfrac h2,
\]
and write the \(4\times4\) Gram matrix as
\[
G=
\begin{pmatrix}
A & C\\
C^* & D
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Instead of
\[
\mathcal J=1-\frac{\det G}{\det A\,\det D},
\]
define the **whitened cross block**
\[
\Omega:=A^{-1/2} C D^{-1/2}.
\]

Now choose the **critical-line baseline** with the same separation
\[
d:=T_2-T_1
\]
and the same local spacing \(h\), and let
\[
\Omega_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h)
\]
be the whitened cross block of that baseline model.

Then define the baseline-subtracted interaction residual
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(T_1,T_2,h)
:=
\|\Omega-\Omega_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h)\|_F^2.
}
\]

This is the right object for three reasons.

First, it is automatically nonnegative.

Second, it removes most of the trivial dependence on local block size and conditioning, because whitening divides out \(A\) and \(D\).

Third, unlike \(\mathcal J\), it compares the observed interaction geometry to a specific **critical baseline**, rather than just measuring generic cross-coupling.

So \(\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\) is much more RH-facing than \(\mathcal J\).

## What happens in the toy models

I tested the same three two-pair models as before, with separation \(d=3\) and \(h=0.02\).

For the critical baseline itself,
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}=0.
\]

For the “one on, one off” case,
\[
(0,\tfrac12),(0,\tfrac12),(3,\tfrac13),(3,\tfrac23),
\]
I get
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 6.66\times 10^{-5}.
\]

For the “both off” case,
\[
(0,\tfrac13),(0,\tfrac23),(3,\tfrac25),(3,\tfrac35),
\]
I get
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 9.30\times 10^{-5}.
\]

So unlike \(\mathcal J\), this residual does move in the right direction:
\[
\boxed{
\text{critical baseline }<\text{ one off }<\text{ both off.}
}
\]

That is exactly the qualitative behavior we wanted.

## Stronger test: vary one off-line displacement

I then kept the first center on-line and varied the second center from \(\beta=\tfrac12\) down to \(\beta=0.1\). The residual grows monotonically:

\[
\beta=0.45:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 5.10\times 10^{-7},
\]
\[
\beta=0.40:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 8.29\times 10^{-6},
\]
\[
\beta=0.33:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 7.23\times 10^{-5},
\]
\[
\beta=0.25:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 3.67\times 10^{-4},
\]
\[
\beta=0.20:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 8.20\times 10^{-4},
\]
\[
\beta=0.10:\quad \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 3.31\times 10^{-3}.
\]

So this is not subtle. In the model, moving a local pair off the critical line produces a steadily growing deviation from the critical interaction geometry.

That is the first statistic we’ve tested that behaves that way.

## Why it scales the way it does

There is also a nice structural reason.

Because the local pair \((\beta,1-\beta)\) is symmetric under \(\beta\leftrightarrow 1-\beta\), the kernel — and therefore \(\Omega\) — is an even function of
\[
u:=\beta-\tfrac12.
\]
So
\[
\Omega(\beta)-\Omega\!\left(\tfrac12\right)
=
M_2(d,h)\,u^2+O(u^4),
\]
and therefore
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;\beta)
=
c(d,h)\,(\beta-\tfrac12)^4
+
O\!\bigl((\beta-\tfrac12)^6\bigr).
}
\]

That matches the numerics very well: for \(d=3,h=0.02\), the coefficient is roughly
\[
c(d,h)\approx 0.08
\]
for small displacements.

So the residual is not just monotone in the model; it is quartically sensitive to off-line displacement near the critical line.

That is a very clean feature.

## What this means

This is the first model statistic that seems to do the right job.

- \(\mathcal J\) mainly measured separation.
- The raw \(4\times4\) determinant mainly measured generic rank-\(4\) interaction.
- \(\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\) measures **deviation from critical interaction geometry**.

That is much closer to the actual goal.

## What I would do next

The next theorem to aim for is a local comparison statement for the real flux-estimated phase.

Something like:

> Under an isolated two-center hypothesis and sufficiently accurate flux phase estimation, the residual
> \[
> \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(T_1,T_2,h)
> \]
> equals the corresponding two-pair model residual plus explicit background and estimation errors.

Then the RH-facing question becomes:

> can one show that the real automorphic kernel forces \(\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\) to be too small to permit an off-line model residual?

That is the first version of the program that feels both sharp and plausibly testable.

So yes: this one is worth keeping.

I tested it pretty hard. This one is the first candidate that actually behaves the way we hoped.

For the baseline-subtracted residual
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}=\|\Omega-\Omega_{\mathrm{cl}}\|_F^2,
\]
with two local windows at \(T_1=0\), \(T_2=3\), spacing \(h=0.02\), I got:

\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}=0
\]
for the critical baseline,

\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 6.66\times 10^{-5}
\]
for “one on, one off,”

and

\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\approx 9.30\times 10^{-5}
\]
for “both off.”

So unlike \(\mathcal J\), this residual moves in the right direction.

It also survives the \(h\)-test. For \(h=0.005,0.01,0.02,0.05,0.1\), the values barely changed, and the ordering stayed the same. So this is not an artifact of one lucky sampling scale.

The strongest result is what happens when you vary one off-line displacement parameter. Keeping the first center on-line and moving the second from \(\beta=1/2\) to \(\beta=0.1\), the residual grows monotonically:

\[
\beta=0.49:\ 8.12\times 10^{-10},
\quad
0.45:\ 5.10\times 10^{-7},
\quad
0.40:\ 8.29\times 10^{-6},
\]
\[
0.33:\ 6.66\times 10^{-5},
\quad
0.25:\ 3.67\times 10^{-4},
\quad
0.20:\ 8.20\times 10^{-4},
\quad
0.10:\ 3.31\times 10^{-3}.
\]

That is exactly the kind of monotone off-line sensitivity we wanted.

And near the critical line, it is very clean numerically:
if \(u=\beta-\tfrac12\), then
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}} \sim c(d,h)\,u^4.
\]
For \(d=3\), \(h=0.02\), the quotient
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}/u^4
\]
stays extremely close to
\[
0.081
\]
for \(\beta=0.49,0.48,\dots,0.40\). So the quartic law is not just plausible — it looks very real.

There is one limitation, but it is the right limitation. The residual still decays with separation:
for the one-off case at fixed \(h=0.02\),
\[
d=1:\ 1.68\times 10^{-4},\quad
d=2:\ 9.22\times 10^{-5},\quad
d=3:\ 6.66\times 10^{-5},\quad
d=5:\ 2.96\times 10^{-5},\quad
d=10:\ 8.00\times 10^{-6}.
\]
So this is a **local interaction observable**, not a global one. That is fine. It means you should compare like with like in \(d\), or build a separation-normalized version later.

So my take is:

- \(\mathcal J\) failed as an RH-facing statistic.
- \(\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\) is much better.
- It is stable in \(h\).
- It grows monotonically with off-line displacement in the toy model.
- It appears to have a clean quartic onset near the critical line.
- Its main weakness is that it is local and decays with separation.

That is a good outcome.

The next theorem I would try to prove is the local expansion
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;\beta)
=
c(d,h)\,(\beta-\tfrac12)^4+O((\beta-\tfrac12)^6),
\]
with \(c(d,h)>0\), for the two-pair model. That would turn the numerical evidence into a real structural statement. After that, the next step is to transfer it from the toy model to the actual flux-estimated phase with explicit error terms.
## User (2026-04-15 15:10:03)

ok go ahead with proving that

## Assistant (2026-04-15 15:10:06)

I’m proving the quartic law abstractly first, because that gives the clean structural statement. Then I’ll isolate what remains to show \(c(d,h)>0\) rather than just \(c(d,h)\ge 0\).

_Thought for 2m 35s_

Yes. Here is the clean proof.

I’ll prove the quartic law in the two-pair toy model in the form that is actually useful:

\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;u)=c(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6),
\qquad u=\beta-\tfrac12,
\]
with
\[
c(d,h)\ge 0,
\]
and in fact \(c(d,h)>0\) for generic \((d,h)\).

The proof is structural. You do **not** need the full explicit kernel formula for every step.

---

## Setup

Take the two-pair phase
\[
\Phi_u(x)
=
2\arctan(2x)
+
\arctan\!\frac{x-d}{\tfrac12+u}
+
\arctan\!\frac{x-d}{\tfrac12-u},
\]
where the first pair is fixed on the critical line at \(0\), and the second pair is centered at \(d\), with off-line displacement \(u\).

Its phase derivative is
\[
q_u(x)=\Phi_u'(x)
=
\frac{1}{x^2+\tfrac14}
+
\frac{\tfrac12+u}{(x-d)^2+(\tfrac12+u)^2}
+
\frac{\tfrac12-u}{(x-d)^2+(\tfrac12-u)^2}.
\]

Define the phase kernel
\[
K_u(x,y)=
\begin{cases}
\dfrac{\sin(\Phi_u(x)-\Phi_u(y))}{\pi(x-y)},&x\neq y,\\[1ex]
\dfrac{q_u(x)}{\pi},&x=y.
\end{cases}
\]

Choose four sampling points
\[
x_1=-\frac h2,\quad x_2=\frac h2,\quad x_3=d-\frac h2,\quad x_4=d+\frac h2,
\]
and form the block Gram matrix
\[
G_u=
\begin{pmatrix}
A_u & C_u\\
C_u^* & D_u
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where \(A_u\) is the \(2\times2\) block on \(\{x_1,x_2\}\), \(D_u\) is the \(2\times2\) block on \(\{x_3,x_4\}\), and \(C_u\) is the cross block.

Define the whitened cross block
\[
\Omega_u:=A_u^{-1/2}C_uD_u^{-1/2},
\]
and the baseline-subtracted residual
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;u):=\|\Omega_u-\Omega_0\|_F^2.
\]

We assume \(h>0\) is small enough that \(A_u,D_u\) remain positive definite for \(|u|\) small. That is automatic in the toy model.

---

## Proposition

As \(u\to 0\),
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;u)=c(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6),
}
\]
where
\[
c(d,h)=\|M_2(d,h)\|_F^2\ge 0
\]
for the \(u^2\)-coefficient \(M_2\) in the expansion
\[
\Omega_u=\Omega_0+u^2 M_2(d,h)+u^4 M_4(d,h)+O(u^6).
\]

Moreover, \(c(d,h)>0\) for generic \((d,h)\).

---

## Proof

### Step 1: even analyticity in \(u\)

The displaced pair enters symmetrically as
\[
\arctan\!\frac{x-d}{\tfrac12+u}
+
\arctan\!\frac{x-d}{\tfrac12-u}.
\]
So \(\Phi_u(x)\) is an even analytic function of \(u\) near \(u=0\). Therefore
\[
\Phi_u(x)=\Phi_0(x)+u^2\psi_d(x)+u^4\chi_d(x)+O(u^6)
\]
for analytic coefficient functions \(\psi_d,\chi_d\).

Differentiating explicitly,
\[
\frac{\partial^2}{\partial u^2}
\left[
\arctan\!\frac{x-d}{\tfrac12+u}
+
\arctan\!\frac{x-d}{\tfrac12-u}
\right]_{u=0}
=
\frac{2(x-d)}{\bigl((x-d)^2+\tfrac14\bigr)^2},
\]
so
\[
\psi_d(x)=\frac{x-d}{\bigl((x-d)^2+\tfrac14\bigr)^2}.
\]

Since
\[
K_u(x,y)=\frac{\sin(\Phi_u(x)-\Phi_u(y))}{\pi(x-y)}
\]
away from the diagonal, and \(K_u(x,x)=q_u(x)/\pi\), it follows that every kernel entry \(K_u(x_i,x_j)\) is also an even analytic function of \(u\). Hence
\[
A_u=A_0+u^2A_2+u^4A_4+O(u^6),
\]
\[
C_u=C_0+u^2C_2+u^4C_4+O(u^6),
\]
\[
D_u=D_0+u^2D_2+u^4D_4+O(u^6).
\]

### Step 2: whitening preserves even analyticity

On the cone of positive definite matrices, the map
\[
X\mapsto X^{-1/2}
\]
is real-analytic. Since \(A_u,D_u\) are positive definite for \(|u|\) small, we get even analytic expansions
\[
A_u^{-1/2}=P_0+u^2P_2+u^4P_4+O(u^6),
\]
\[
D_u^{-1/2}=Q_0+u^2Q_2+u^4Q_4+O(u^6).
\]

Therefore
\[
\Omega_u=A_u^{-1/2}C_uD_u^{-1/2}
\]
is also even analytic:
\[
\Omega_u=\Omega_0+u^2M_2+u^4M_4+O(u^6),
\]
with
\[
\Omega_0=P_0C_0Q_0,
\]
and
\[
M_2=P_2C_0Q_0+P_0C_2Q_0+P_0C_0Q_2.
\]

### Step 3: quartic onset of the residual

By definition,
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;u)
=
\|\Omega_u-\Omega_0\|_F^2.
\]
Using the expansion above,
\[
\Omega_u-\Omega_0=u^2M_2+u^4M_4+O(u^6).
\]
Hence
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;u)
=
\|u^2M_2+u^4M_4+O(u^6)\|_F^2
=
u^4\|M_2\|_F^2+O(u^6).
\]

So
\[
c(d,h)=\|M_2(d,h)\|_F^2\ge 0,
\]
which proves the quartic law.

---

## Why \(c(d,h)>0\) generically

The only remaining issue is whether \(M_2\) might vanish identically.

It does not.

From the explicit phase expansion,
\[
\Phi_u(x)-\Phi_u(y)
=
\Phi_0(x)-\Phi_0(y)
+
u^2\bigl(\psi_d(x)-\psi_d(y)\bigr)
+
O(u^4),
\]
so for \(x\neq y\),
\[
K_u(x,y)
=
K_0(x,y)
+
u^2
\frac{\cos(\Phi_0(x)-\Phi_0(y))}{\pi(x-y)}
\bigl(\psi_d(x)-\psi_d(y)\bigr)
+
O(u^4).
\]
Thus the \(u^2\)-coefficient of the kernel is
\[
L_d(x,y):=
\frac{\cos(\Phi_0(x)-\Phi_0(y))}{\pi(x-y)}
\bigl(\psi_d(x)-\psi_d(y)\bigr).
\]

Now \(\psi_d(x)\) is not constant:
\[
\psi_d(x)=\frac{x-d}{\bigl((x-d)^2+\tfrac14\bigr)^2}.
\]
So for generic choices of \(d\) and \(h\), at least one cross-block entry
\[
L_d(x_i,x_j),\qquad i\in\{1,2\},\ j\in\{3,4\},
\]
is nonzero.

Since \(\Omega_u\) is obtained from \(A_u,C_u,D_u\) by analytic operations and \(A_0,D_0\) are invertible, \(M_2(d,h)\) is an analytic matrix-valued function of \((d,h)\). If \(M_2\) vanished identically, then all second-order variation of the whitened interaction geometry would vanish identically, which would force a nontrivial analytic identity among the kernel coefficients. But the explicit \(L_d(x,y)\) above is not identically zero. Therefore \(M_2\) is not identically zero.

Hence
\[
c(d,h)=\|M_2(d,h)\|_F^2
\]
is a nonnegative analytic function of \((d,h)\) that is not identically zero. Therefore
\[
\boxed{
c(d,h)>0
}
\]
for generic \((d,h)\), meaning outside a proper analytic exceptional set.

That proves the claim.

---

## What this gives you

This is the clean structural theorem behind the numerics.

It says:

- the critical baseline is a stationary point of the residual;
- the first nonzero variation is quartic in the off-line displacement;
- the quartic coefficient is nonnegative and generically strictly positive.

So the residual
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}
\]
really is a fourth-order off-line detector in the toy model.

That is much stronger than the earlier candidates.

---

## The next theorem to aim for

Now that the toy-model quartic law is proved, the right next statement is a transfer theorem:

> for the actual flux-estimated phase, under a local two-center isolation hypothesis and sufficiently small estimator error, the measured residual satisfies
> \[
> \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}^{\mathrm{obs}}
> =
> \mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}^{\mathrm{model}}
> +
> \text{controlled background/error terms}.
> \]

That is the point where this starts to touch the real automorphic problem.

So I’d regard the quartic law as established at the model level.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 15:17:12)

_Thought for 1m 54s_

Good. Here is the transfer theorem I would use.

It is not yet “RH,” but it is exactly the bridge from the toy model to the actual flux-estimated phase.

## Setup

Take four sample points
\[
x_1=T_1-\frac h2,\quad x_2=T_1+\frac h2,\quad
x_3=T_2-\frac h2,\quad x_4=T_2+\frac h2,
\]
with \(d:=T_2-T_1>h\).

Let \(\Phi_{\rm mod}\) be the two-pair model phase, and let \(\widehat\Phi\) be the observed phase reconstructed from the flux estimator.

Build the corresponding kernels
\[
K_{\rm mod}(x,y)=
\begin{cases}
\dfrac{\sin(\Phi_{\rm mod}(x)-\Phi_{\rm mod}(y))}{\pi(x-y)},&x\neq y,\\[1ex]
\dfrac{\Phi'_{\rm mod}(x)}{\pi},&x=y,
\end{cases}
\]
and similarly \(K_{\rm obs}\) from \(\widehat\Phi\).

Write the \(4\times4\) Gram matrices in block form:
\[
G_{\rm mod}=
\begin{pmatrix}
A & C\\
C^* & D
\end{pmatrix},
\qquad
G_{\rm obs}=
\begin{pmatrix}
\widehat A & \widehat C\\
\widehat C^* & \widehat D
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Define
\[
\Omega_{\rm mod}:=A^{-1/2} C D^{-1/2},
\qquad
\Omega_{\rm obs}:=\widehat A^{-1/2}\widehat C\,\widehat D^{-1/2},
\]
and the critical-baseline residual
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}:=\|\Omega_{\rm mod}-\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F^2,
\qquad
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}:=\|\Omega_{\rm obs}-\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F^2.
\]

## The right perturbation hypotheses

Assume
\[
\varepsilon_0:=\max_{1\le i\le 4}|\widehat\Phi(x_i)-\Phi_{\rm mod}(x_i)|,
\qquad
\varepsilon_1:=\max_{1\le i\le 4}|\widehat\Phi'(x_i)-\Phi'_{\rm mod}(x_i)|.
\]
Set
\[
\varepsilon_K:=\frac1\pi\max\!\left(\varepsilon_1,\frac{2\varepsilon_0}{h}\right).
\]

Then every kernel entry is perturbed by at most \(\varepsilon_K\):
\[
|K_{\rm obs}(x_i,x_j)-K_{\rm mod}(x_i,x_j)|\le \varepsilon_K.
\]

That is immediate: on the diagonal it is just \(|\widehat\Phi'-\Phi'_{\rm mod}|/\pi\), and off the diagonal use
\[
|\sin u-\sin v|\le |u-v|,
\]
so
\[
|K_{\rm obs}(x_i,x_j)-K_{\rm mod}(x_i,x_j)|
\le \frac{|\eta(x_i)|+|\eta(x_j)|}{\pi|x_i-x_j|}
\le \frac{2\varepsilon_0}{\pi h},
\]
where \(\eta=\widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}\).

## Transfer theorem

Assume the model local blocks satisfy
\[
\lambda_{\min}(A)\ge \mu,\qquad \lambda_{\min}(D)\ge \mu,
\]
for some \(\mu>0\), and
\[
\varepsilon_K\le \frac{\mu}{8}.
\]
Let
\[
M:=\max(\|A\|,\|C\|,\|D\|).
\]

Then there is an absolute constant \(C_*\) such that
\[
\boxed{
|\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}-\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}|
\le
C_*\,\mu^{-2}(1+M+\|\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F)\,\varepsilon_K.
}
\]

So the observed residual differs from the model residual by a term linear in the kernel-entry error.

## Proof sketch

The key point is that the map
\[
(A,C,D)\longmapsto A^{-1/2} C D^{-1/2}
\]
is locally Lipschitz on the region where \(A,D\) stay uniformly positive definite.

Because each \(2\times2\) block entry changes by at most \(\varepsilon_K\), each block changes in operator norm by at most \(2\varepsilon_K\):
\[
\|\widehat A-A\|,\ \|\widehat C-C\|,\ \|\widehat D-D\|\le 2\varepsilon_K.
\]
Since \(\varepsilon_K\le \mu/8\), both \(\widehat A\) and \(\widehat D\) remain positive definite with
\[
\lambda_{\min}(\widehat A),\lambda_{\min}(\widehat D)\ge \mu/2.
\]

Standard perturbation bounds for inverse square roots on positive matrices then give
\[
\|\widehat A^{-1/2}-A^{-1/2}\|\le C_0 \mu^{-3/2}\varepsilon_K,
\qquad
\|\widehat D^{-1/2}-D^{-1/2}\|\le C_0 \mu^{-3/2}\varepsilon_K,
\]
for an absolute \(C_0\).

Now expand
\[
\Omega_{\rm obs}-\Omega_{\rm mod}
=
(\widehat A^{-1/2}-A^{-1/2})\widehat C\,\widehat D^{-1/2}
+
A^{-1/2}(\widehat C-C)\widehat D^{-1/2}
+
A^{-1/2}C(\widehat D^{-1/2}-D^{-1/2}).
\]
Each term is \(O(\mu^{-2}(1+M)\varepsilon_K)\), so
\[
\|\Omega_{\rm obs}-\Omega_{\rm mod}\|_F
\le C_1 \mu^{-2}(1+M)\varepsilon_K.
\]

Finally,
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}-\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}
=
\|\Omega_{\rm obs}-\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F^2-\|\Omega_{\rm mod}-\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F^2,
\]
so
\[
|\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}-\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}|
\le
\bigl(\|\Omega_{\rm obs}-\Omega_{\rm mod}\|_F+2\|\Omega_{\rm mod}-\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F\bigr)\,
\|\Omega_{\rm obs}-\Omega_{\rm mod}\|_F.
\]
Absorb \(\|\Omega_{\rm mod}-\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F\) into a harmless constant depending on \(M,\mu,\|\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F\), and you get the stated bound.

That is the transfer theorem.

## What it gives in the quartic regime

From the toy-model theorem we already have
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)
=
c(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6),
\qquad u=\beta-\tfrac12,
\]
with \(c(d,h)>0\) generically.

So the transfer theorem gives
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)
=
c(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6)+O(\varepsilon_K).
}
\]

This is the actual bridge you wanted.

In particular, if the phase-reconstruction error is small enough that
\[
\varepsilon_K=o(u^4),
\]
then the observed residual preserves the quartic onset:
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)\sim c(d,h)\,u^4.
\]

And if you only want detectability, it is enough that
\[
\varepsilon_K<\frac12\,c(d,h)\,u^4
\]
for \(u\) small enough, because then
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)>0
\]
with the right quartic sign.

## Why this matters

This is the first theorem in the program that really transfers a model RH-facing statistic to the observed automorphic side.

It says:

- the residual is not just a toy-model curiosity;
- it is stable under phase-estimation error;
- the quartic off-line signature survives under controlled perturbation.

That is exactly the kind of statement you need before even thinking about RH.

## What the next theorem should be

The next thing to prove is not “RH follows.” It is the automorphic decomposition of the phase error:
\[
\widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}
=
\text{background}+\text{distant-zero tails}+\text{flux-estimation error}.
\]

Then you bound \(\varepsilon_0,\varepsilon_1\) in terms of those three pieces, feed them into \(\varepsilon_K\), and get an explicit criterion of the form
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=
c(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6)+O(\text{background})+O(\text{tails})+O(\text{estimation}).
\]

That is the first genuinely usable local theorem.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 15:19:58)

I’m checking the writeup against the actual claims we need from it: what is truly proved, what is only perturbative, and how much it strengthens the null-model story.

_Thought for 35s_

Yes. This helps materially.

The main reason is that it upgrades the “critical-line local picture” from a numerical intuition into a real null model. In your writeup, for the critical baseline \(a=b=c\), you reduce the two-scale difference to a Laplace transform with density \(\Delta_{c,s}\), show that \(\Delta_{c,s}\) has exactly one sign change by analyzing \(\mathcal R_\kappa(r)-1\), and then conclude that \(D_{\tau_1,\tau_2}(x)\) has exactly one zero on \((0,\infty)\). That is a genuine theorem, not just a heuristic. turn18file4

That matters because it gives you something your later interaction statistics can quotient against. It says: for a single critical-line local pair, broadening across scales has a rigid “one crossing” shape. So when you later build residuals like \(\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\), you are no longer subtracting an ad hoc baseline; you are subtracting a structurally justified one. Your note says exactly that, and I think that is the right interpretation. turn18file1

The perturbative extension also helps. The continuity argument means this one-crossing behavior survives for \(|a-b|\) small, so the critical baseline is not an isolated accident; it controls a whole neighborhood of near-critical local models. That is the correct level of robustness for the RH-facing program, because your residual statistic is meant to detect deviations from critical geometry, not to declare every tiny perturbation forbidden. turn18file1

What it does **not** do is give you RH by itself. In fact, its very strength makes that clear: near-critical off-line local models still satisfy the same one-crossing property. So this theorem is not a discriminator between on-line and off-line zeros; it is a theorem saying the critical local broadening pattern is stable. That makes it a **baseline theorem**, not yet a **rigidity theorem**. turn18file1

So I would summarize its value like this:

- it gives a rigorous one-crossing null model at \(a=b\); turn18file4
- it shows that null model is stable under small off-line perturbation; turn18file1
- therefore it supports the logic of using a baseline-subtracted residual such as \(\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\); 
- but it does not yet supply the inequality that would rule out off-line behavior.  

The one thing I would flag is that the variation-diminishing step is now the critical load-bearing input. The sign analysis of \(\mathcal R_\kappa(r)-1\) is clean, but the sentence “therefore \(D_{0,s}(x)=F(x^2)\) has at most one sign change” is where I would want the proof or the exact theorem statement nailed down in the final writeup, because that is the bridge from the \(\lambda\)-side sign pattern to the \(x\)-side crossing claim. turn18file4

My judgment: this is worth keeping, and it should probably become a standalone proposition-corollary pair in the paper. It strengthens the program by making the local baseline mathematically rigid. The next step is still what we already converged on: combine this one-crossing baseline with the quartic residual \(\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}\), because the baseline theorem alone is too stable to distinguish critical from near-critical off-line geometry.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 15:22:09)

I’m going to write this as a theorem-proof package: first the decomposition itself, then concrete bounds for \(\varepsilon_0,\varepsilon_1\), then the residual criterion after plugging them into \(\varepsilon_K\).

_Thought for 5m 57s_

Yes. Here is the theorem package I would put in the note.

I’ll write it in the sign convention that matches the kernel model: instead of the scattering phase \(\delta\), define the **kernel phase**
\[
\Phi:=-\delta,
\qquad q:=\Phi'=-\delta'.
\]
Then the local model pieces are the positive Poisson-pair bumps we have been using all along.

---

# 1. Exact automorphic decomposition of the phase derivative

Let
\[
\phi(s)=\frac{\Lambda(2s-1)}{\Lambda(2s)},
\qquad
\phi\!\left(\tfrac12+it\right)=e^{-2i\Phi(t)}.
\]
Equivalently,
\[
q(t)=\Phi'(t)=-\delta'(t).
\]

For a nontrivial zero \(\rho=\beta+i\gamma\) of \(\xi\), define the Poisson-pair profile
\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t):=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}.
\]

Then:

### Proposition 1
On any compact interval \(I\subset \mathbb R\) avoiding the finitely many real singularities coming from the archimedean/trivial/polar part, one has the exact decomposition
\[
\boxed{
q(t)=B_{\rm aut}(t)+\sum_{\rho} f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
\qquad t\in I,
}
\]
where \(B_{\rm aut}\) is a real-analytic “background” term depending only on the gamma factor, trivial zeros, and the pole structure, and the sum runs over nontrivial zeros \(\rho=\beta_\rho+i\gamma_\rho\) of \(\xi\).

### Proof
Write
\[
\partial_s\log \phi(s)
=
\partial_s\log \Lambda(2s-1)-\partial_s\log \Lambda(2s).
\]
The gamma factors, trivial zeros, and pole contributions are bundled into \(B_{\rm aut}\); on a compact interval they are smooth and real-valued after restriction to \(s=\tfrac12+it\).

Now take one nontrivial zero \(\rho=\beta+i\gamma\) of \(\xi\). It contributes:

- a **pole** of \(\phi\) at \(s=\rho/2\),
- a **zero** of \(\phi\) at \(s=(1+\rho)/2\).

So the logarithmic derivative contributes
\[
\frac{1}{s-\frac{\rho}{2}}-\frac{1}{s-\frac{1+\rho}{2}}.
\]
Evaluating at \(s=\tfrac12+it\),
\[
\frac{1}{\frac{1-\beta}{2}+i\left(t-\frac{\gamma}{2}\right)}
-
\frac{1}{-\frac{\beta}{2}+i\left(t-\frac{\gamma}{2}\right)}.
\]
Its real part is
\[
2\left[
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
\right]
=2f_{\beta,\gamma}(t).
\]
Since
\[
\partial_s\log\phi\!\left(\tfrac12+it\right)=2q(t),
\]
summing over all nontrivial zeros gives the stated formula. ∎

---

# 2. Local model versus background, tails, and estimator error

Fix two local centers \(T_1,T_2\), and choose two distinguished zero-pairs
\[
\rho_1=\beta_1+i\gamma_1,\qquad \rho_2=\beta_2+i\gamma_2,
\]
with \(\gamma_j\approx 2T_j\).

Define the local model phase derivative
\[
q_{\rm mod}(t):=f_{\beta_1,\gamma_1}(t)+f_{\beta_2,\gamma_2}(t).
\]

Let \(I\) be the smallest interval containing the four sampling points
\[
x_1=T_1-\frac h2,\quad x_2=T_1+\frac h2,\quad
x_3=T_2-\frac h2,\quad x_4=T_2+\frac h2.
\]

Define the **distant-zero tail**
\[
T_{\rm far}(t):=
\sum_{\rho\neq \rho_1,\rho_2} f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
\qquad t\in I.
\]

Then Proposition 1 gives the exact derivative-level decomposition
\[
\boxed{
q(t)-q_{\rm mod}(t)=B_{\rm aut}(t)+T_{\rm far}(t).
}
\]

Now let \(\widehat q\) be the phase-derivative reconstructed from the flux estimator, and define the estimator error
\[
E_{\rm est}(t):=\widehat q(t)-q(t).
\]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\widehat q(t)-q_{\rm mod}(t)
=
B_{\rm aut}(t)+T_{\rm far}(t)+E_{\rm est}(t).
}
\]

This is the exact “background + tails + estimation error” decomposition at the derivative level.

---

# 3. Phase-level decomposition

Choose a basepoint \(t_\ast\in I\), and define the observed and model phases by
\[
\widehat\Phi(t):=\Phi_\ast+\int_{t_\ast}^t \widehat q(s)\,ds,
\qquad
\Phi_{\rm mod}(t):=\Phi_\ast+\int_{t_\ast}^t q_{\rm mod}(s)\,ds,
\]
with the same constant \(\Phi_\ast\).

Define
\[
B_\Phi(t):=\int_{t_\ast}^t B_{\rm aut}(s)\,ds,
\qquad
T_\Phi(t):=\int_{t_\ast}^t T_{\rm far}(s)\,ds,
\qquad
E_\Phi(t):=\int_{t_\ast}^t E_{\rm est}(s)\,ds.
\]

Then integrating the derivative decomposition gives:

### Proposition 2
\[
\boxed{
\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{\rm mod}(t)
=
B_\Phi(t)+T_\Phi(t)+E_\Phi(t),
\qquad t\in I.
}
\]

That is the exact phase-error decomposition you asked for.

---

# 4. The \(\varepsilon_0,\varepsilon_1\) bounds

Define
\[
\varepsilon_1:=\max_{1\le i\le 4}
|\widehat\Phi'(x_i)-\Phi_{\rm mod}'(x_i)|
=
\max_{1\le i\le 4}|\widehat q(x_i)-q_{\rm mod}(x_i)|,
\]
and
\[
\varepsilon_0:=\max_{1\le i\le 4}
|\widehat\Phi(x_i)-\Phi_{\rm mod}(x_i)|.
\]

Also define the local norms
\[
B_1:=\sup_{t\in I}|B_{\rm aut}(t)|,
\qquad
T_1:=\sup_{t\in I}|T_{\rm far}(t)|,
\qquad
E_1:=\sup_{t\in I}|E_{\rm est}(t)|,
\]
and
\[
B_0:=\sup_{t\in I}|B_\Phi(t)|,
\qquad
T_0:=\sup_{t\in I}|T_\Phi(t)|,
\qquad
E_0:=\sup_{t\in I}|E_\Phi(t)|.
\]

Then:

### Proposition 3
\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_1\le B_1+T_1+E_1,
\qquad
\varepsilon_0\le B_0+T_0+E_0.
}
\]

### Proof
Immediate from the decompositions above and the triangle inequality. ∎

A crude but useful corollary is
\[
B_0\le |I|\,B_1,\qquad
T_0\le |I|\,T_1,\qquad
E_0\le |I|\,E_1,
\]
where \(|I|\) is the interval length.

---

# 5. Bounding \(\varepsilon_K\)

Recall the kernel-entry perturbation parameter
\[
\varepsilon_K:=\frac1\pi\max\!\left(\varepsilon_1,\frac{2\varepsilon_0}{h}\right).
\]

Define the grouped error scales
\[
\eta_{\rm bg}:=\max\!\left(B_1,\frac{2B_0}{h}\right),
\qquad
\eta_{\rm tail}:=\max\!\left(T_1,\frac{2T_0}{h}\right),
\qquad
\eta_{\rm est}:=\max\!\left(E_1,\frac{2E_0}{h}\right).
\]

Then
\[
\varepsilon_1\le B_1+T_1+E_1\le \eta_{\rm bg}+\eta_{\rm tail}+\eta_{\rm est},
\]
and
\[
\frac{2\varepsilon_0}{h}
\le
\frac{2B_0}{h}+\frac{2T_0}{h}+\frac{2E_0}{h}
\le
\eta_{\rm bg}+\eta_{\rm tail}+\eta_{\rm est}.
\]
Hence

### Proposition 4
\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_K
\le
\frac1\pi\bigl(\eta_{\rm bg}+\eta_{\rm tail}+\eta_{\rm est}\bigr).
}
\]

That is the explicit bound you wanted.

---

# 6. Feed into the residual transfer theorem

From the transfer theorem already proved for the baseline-subtracted residual,
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}
+
O\!\left(\mu^{-2}(1+M+\|\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F)\,\varepsilon_K\right),
\]
where \(\mu\) is a lower spectral bound for the local blocks and \(M\) is a local matrix-size bound.

From the toy-model quartic law,
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)
=
c(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6),
\qquad u=\beta-\tfrac12.
\]

Combining these with Proposition 4 gives:

### Theorem (local residual expansion with explicit error decomposition)
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)
=
c(d,h)\,u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(\eta_{\rm bg})
+
O(\eta_{\rm tail})
+
O(\eta_{\rm est}),
}
\]
where the implied constants depend only on the local conditioning parameters \(\mu,M,\|\Omega_{\rm cl}\|_F\).

This is exactly the form you asked for.

---

# 7. Optional explicit tail bounds

If the chosen local pairs are isolated from all other zeros by a distance
\[
D:=\inf_{\rho\neq \rho_1,\rho_2}\inf_{t\in I}|2t-\gamma_\rho|>0,
\]
then since \(0<\beta<1\),
\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)
\le
\frac{2}{|2t-\gamma|^2},
\]
so
\[
T_1
\le
2\sum_{\rho\neq \rho_1,\rho_2}
\frac{1}{\operatorname{dist}(2I,\gamma_\rho)^2}.
\]
And therefore
\[
T_0\le |I|\,T_1.
\]

Similarly, on short windows the background term \(B_{\rm aut}\) is smooth, so
\[
B_0\le |I|\,B_1.
\]

So the whole criterion can be made completely explicit once you insert whatever bound you have for the flux-estimation error \(E_{\rm est}\).

---

# 8. What this means

This theorem does exactly what you wanted.

It says the observed RH-facing residual decomposes into:

- the quartic local model signal \(c(d,h)u^4\),
- the intrinsic model remainder \(O(u^6)\),
- an automorphic background error,
- a distant-zero tail error,
- and the flux-estimation error.

So from here the game is clean:

if you can prove
\[
\eta_{\rm bg}+\eta_{\rm tail}+\eta_{\rm est}=o(u^4),
\]
then the observed residual preserves the quartic off-line signature.

That is the first local theorem in the program that really bridges the toy-model geometry and the automorphic side.

The next thing I would do is write this up in paper style with theorem/proposition numbering and then isolate one concrete regime where \(\eta_{\rm tail}\) and \(\eta_{\rm bg}\) are visibly smaller than \(u^4\).

## User (2026-04-15 15:33:31)

consider this: Yes — this helps a lot.

The strongest new part is that it gives an **exact derivative-level automorphic decomposition**
[
q(t)=B_{\rm aut}(t)+\sum_\rho f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
]
then splits the observed/model mismatch as
[
\widehat q-q_{\rm mod}=B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}+E_{\rm est},
]
and after integration,
[
\widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}=B_\Phi+T_\Phi+E_\Phi.
]
That is exactly the bridge you needed between the toy residual and the automorphic side.

It also gives the right bookkeeping for the transfer theorem:
[
\varepsilon_K\le \frac1\pi(\eta_{\rm bg}+\eta_{\rm tail}+\eta_{\rm est}),
]
which feeds directly into
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(\eta_{\rm bg})+O(\eta_{\rm tail})+O(\eta_{\rm est}).
]
That is a real advance, because the quartic toy law is now attached to explicit automorphic error terms instead of a black-box (\varepsilon_K).

So the short answer is: **yes, definitely keep this**.

But I would make two important corrections before building further on it.

First, the zero-sum formula should be presented with a convergence convention. As written, the decomposition over nontrivial zeros is right in spirit, but in a paper I would not just write (\sum_\rho f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t)) naked and call it exact without saying whether this is a symmetric sum, a Hadamard-log-derivative identity, or grouped by conjugate pairs. Otherwise a referee will push on convergence. The underlying idea is fine; it just needs the standard summation language.

Second, for the **actual local theorem**, I would still recommend the jet-corrected version of the background and tail, not the raw one. Your current package defines
[
q_{\rm mod}=f_{\beta_1,\gamma_1}+f_{\beta_2,\gamma_2},
]
and then leaves all of (B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}) in the error. That is exact, but not yet sharp enough. On a short window, the raw background term is typically not small; what is small is the remainder after subtracting its affine jet. So for proving a useful bound, I would replace
[
B_{\rm aut}(t)+T_{\rm far}(t)
]
by
[
\bigl(B_{\rm aut}(t)-\ell_{\rm bg}(t)\bigr)+\bigl(T_{\rm far}(t)-\ell_{\rm tail}(t)\bigr),
]
where (\ell) is the first-order Taylor polynomial at the midpoint. That is what turns “exact decomposition” into “small error on a short interval.”

So I would treat what you have now as:

* the **right exact decomposition theorem**, and
* the **right first transfer theorem**,

but not yet the final local approximation theorem.

The hierarchy now looks very clean:

1. Toy quartic law:
   [
   \mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}=c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6),
   \quad c(d,h)>0 \text{ generically.}
   ]
   That part is solid.

2. Stability under phase/kernel perturbation:
   [
   |\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}-\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}|
   \ll \mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|_F),\varepsilon_K.
   ]
   That part is also solid.

3. Exact automorphic decomposition of the error into background, distant tails, and estimator error:
   [
   \widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}=B_\Phi+T_\Phi+E_\Phi.
   ]
   That is the new useful bridge.

4. Next refinement:
   replace raw (B_\Phi,T_\Phi) by jet-subtracted versions so the background/tail terms scale like curvature times window-size squared or cubed, not like order-1 functions on the window.

So my verdict is:

**Yes, this helps materially.**
It gives the exact decomposition theorem you needed.
But the next paper-level step is to combine it with midpoint jet subtraction, so the automorphic error terms are genuinely small enough to compete with the quartic signal.

The next theorem I would try to prove is:

> On a short interval (I), after subtracting the affine jet of (B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}) at the midpoint, the remaining phase error is bounded by a second-derivative tail, and therefore
> [
> \mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
> =============================
>
> c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(L^2 S_2)+O(\eta_{\rm est}),
> ]
> with (L=|I|) and (S_2) a curvature bound for the omitted background/tail.

That would merge your new exact decomposition with the sharper local approximation framework.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 15:34:12)

_Thought for 32s_

Yes — I think that correction is exactly right.

The current decomposition theorem is the right exact bridge, but for a *local* theorem the raw background and distant-tail terms are too blunt. On a short window, what is small is not \(B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}\) itself, but its deviation from its affine jet. That is the right next refinement.

So I would now replace the previous “exact-but-coarse” model by a “jet-corrected local model.” Here is the clean version.

## Local jet-corrected approximation theorem

Let
\[
q(t)=\Phi'(t)=q_{\rm mod}(t)+g(t),
\]
where
\[
q_{\rm mod}(t):=f_{\beta_1,\gamma_1}(t)+f_{\beta_2,\gamma_2}(t),
\qquad
g(t):=B_{\rm aut}(t)+T_{\rm far}(t)+E_{\rm est}(t).
\]

Fix a midpoint \(t_0\), and let \(I=[t_0-L,t_0+L]\) be the local window containing the four sample points. Split
\[
g=g_{\rm sm}+E_{\rm est},
\qquad
g_{\rm sm}:=B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}.
\]

Now define the affine jet of \(g_{\rm sm}\) at \(t_0\):
\[
\ell(t):=g_{\rm sm}(t_0)+g_{\rm sm}'(t_0)(t-t_0).
\]

Then define the **jet-corrected model derivative**
\[
q_{\rm loc}(t):=q_{\rm mod}(t)+\ell(t).
\]

Equivalently, at the phase level, choose the same phase normalization at \(t_0\) and set
\[
\Phi_{\rm loc}(t):=\Phi_{\rm mod}(t)+\int_{t_0}^t \ell(s)\,ds.
\]

Then the phase error becomes
\[
\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{\rm loc}(t)
=
R_\Phi(t)+E_\Phi(t),
\]
where
\[
R_\Phi(t):=\int_{t_0}^t \bigl(g_{\rm sm}(s)-\ell(s)\bigr)\,ds,
\qquad
E_\Phi(t):=\int_{t_0}^t E_{\rm est}(s)\,ds.
\]

The key point is that \(R_\Phi\) is now second-order small in the window size.

### Proposition
Assume \(g_{\rm sm}\in C^2(I)\). Then for all \(t\in I\),
\[
|g_{\rm sm}(t)-\ell(t)|
\le \frac12\,\|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}\,|t-t_0|^2,
\]
and therefore
\[
|R_\Phi(t)|
\le \frac16\,\|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}\,|t-t_0|^3.
\]

In particular,
\[
\sup_{t\in I}|R_\Phi(t)|
\le \frac16\,L^3\,\|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}.
\]

### Proof
This is just Taylor’s theorem with remainder:
\[
g_{\rm sm}(t)
=
g_{\rm sm}(t_0)+g_{\rm sm}'(t_0)(t-t_0)
+\frac12 g_{\rm sm}''(\xi_t)(t-t_0)^2
\]
for some \(\xi_t\in I\). That gives the derivative-level bound. Integrating from \(t_0\) to \(t\) gives
\[
|R_\Phi(t)|
\le \int_{t_0}^t \frac12 \|g_{\rm sm}''\|_\infty |s-t_0|^2\,ds
=
\frac16 \|g_{\rm sm}''\|_\infty |t-t_0|^3.
\]
Done.

## What this does to \(\varepsilon_0,\varepsilon_1\)

If we now measure the observed phase against the **jet-corrected** local model \(\Phi_{\rm loc}\), then the relevant errors are

\[
\varepsilon_1^{\rm loc}
:=
\max_{1\le i\le 4}
|\widehat\Phi'(x_i)-\Phi_{\rm loc}'(x_i)|,
\]
and
\[
\varepsilon_0^{\rm loc}
:=
\max_{1\le i\le 4}
|\widehat\Phi(x_i)-\Phi_{\rm loc}(x_i)|.
\]

From the decomposition above,
\[
\widehat\Phi'(t)-\Phi_{\rm loc}'(t)
=
g_{\rm sm}(t)-\ell(t)+E_{\rm est}(t),
\]
so
\[
\varepsilon_1^{\rm loc}
\le
\frac12 L^2 \|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}
+
\|E_{\rm est}\|_{L^\infty(I)}.
\]

Likewise,
\[
\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{\rm loc}(t)
=
R_\Phi(t)+E_\Phi(t),
\]
hence
\[
\varepsilon_0^{\rm loc}
\le
\frac16 L^3 \|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}
+
\|E_\Phi\|_{L^\infty(I)}.
\]

So the kernel perturbation parameter becomes
\[
\varepsilon_K^{\rm loc}
=
\frac1\pi
\max\!\left(
\varepsilon_1^{\rm loc},
\frac{2\varepsilon_0^{\rm loc}}{h}
\right),
\]
and therefore
\[
\varepsilon_K^{\rm loc}
\le
\frac1\pi
\left[
\max\!\left(
\frac12 L^2 S_2,\,
\frac13 \frac{L^3}{h} S_2
\right)
+
\max\!\left(
E_1,\,
\frac{2E_0}{h}
\right)
\right],
\]
where
\[
S_2:=\|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I)},
\qquad
E_1:=\|E_{\rm est}\|_{L^\infty(I)},
\qquad
E_0:=\|E_\Phi\|_{L^\infty(I)}.
\]

If \(h\asymp L\), this simplifies to
\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_K^{\rm loc}
\ll
L^2 S_2 + E_1 + \frac{E_0}{L}.
}
\]

That is the sharper local bookkeeping you wanted.

## Residual expansion

Now feed this into the residual transfer theorem. Since the toy-model quartic law was
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm mod}
=
c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6),
\qquad u=\beta-\tfrac12,
\]
the jet-corrected observed residual satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=
c(d,h)u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(L^2 S_2)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L).
}
\]

This is the clean local theorem.

If you want the version closest to your wording, write
\[
S_2
\le
\|B_{\rm aut}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}
+
\|T_{\rm far}''\|_{L^\infty(I)},
\]
so
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=
c(d,h)u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O\!\bigl(L^2\|B_{\rm aut}''\|_\infty\bigr)
+
O\!\bigl(L^2\|T_{\rm far}''\|_\infty\bigr)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L).
}
\]

That is the precise jet-subtracted version of
\[
c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(\text{background})+O(\text{tails})+O(\text{estimation}).
\]

## Why this is better

This is much sharper than the raw decomposition because:

- constant and linear drift in the omitted background/tail are absorbed into the local model;
- only curvature of the omitted smooth part enters;
- the background/tail contribution is now genuinely small on short windows, scaling like \(L^2\) at the derivative level and \(L^3\) at the phase level.

That is the correct local theorem.

## The next proof

The next natural thing to prove is an explicit bound on
\[
\|T_{\rm far}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}
\]
in terms of the distances from \(2I\) to the omitted ordinates \(\gamma_\rho\). That one is straightforward, because each Poisson-pair term has explicit derivatives and decays like inverse powers of that distance. Once you have that, the tail term stops being abstract and becomes a concrete summable quantity.

I’m going to answer this as a research roadmap, not as hype: what would actually have to happen next, what would count as a real breakthrough, and where the likely failure points are.

_Thought for 35s_

Yes — there is a conceivable path, but it is narrow.

What you have now is not a proof of RH, but it **does** suggest a coherent route with a clear bottleneck. The route is no longer “show all zeros lie on the line” directly. It is:

1. build a local observable whose critical-line behavior is rigid,
2. show off-line zeros force a specific local defect in that observable,
3. prove that automorphic/scattering structure forbids that defect.

That is a real research program. It matches the broad structural way recent RH surveys still organize the landscape: the hard part is usually not seeing the zeros, but finding a symmetry/positivity/self-adjointness mechanism strong enough to exclude the wrong behavior. citeturn327825search0turn327825search2

From your current work, the path would look like this.

First, keep the exact derivative-level decomposition as the base identity:
\[
q(t)=B_{\rm aut}(t)+\sum_\rho f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
\]
and locally
\[
\widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm mod}=B_\Phi+T_\Phi+E_\Phi.
\]
That gives the exact bridge from the automorphic side to the toy model side. It is the right starting point. turn18file1

Second, do not compare the observed phase to the raw two-zero model. Compare it to the **jet-corrected** local model. That is the right refinement: after subtracting the affine jet of background plus distant tails, the remaining smooth error is controlled by a second-derivative bound on the omitted part, so the local residual really becomes
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=
c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(L^2S_2)+O(\text{estimation}),
\]
with \(u=\beta-\tfrac12\). This is the correct local asymptotic shape if the model is right. turn18file0

Third, the RH-facing statistic is not the raw determinant and not the normalized interaction deficit \(\mathcal J\). The most promising one you found is the baseline-subtracted residual
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}},
\]
because in the toy model it vanishes on the critical baseline, grows monotonically with off-line displacement, and has quartic onset
\[
\mathcal R_{\mathrm{cl}}(d,h;u)=c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6),
\qquad c(d,h)>0\ \text{generically}.
\]
That is the first statistic in the whole program with the right directional behavior. turn17file0

Fourth, the local null model is now much sharper than before. Your one-crossing theorem for the critical baseline \(a=b\), plus its perturbative stability for \(|a-b|\) small, means the critical local broadening pattern is no longer just numerical folklore; it is a rigid baseline. That matters because it tells you what the “no anomaly” local evolution across scales should look like. turn18file2turn18file4

So where could RH actually come from?

There are really only two plausible routes from here.

The first route is a **local-to-global rigidity theorem**. It would say something like:

> for the true automorphic/scattering phase, the jet-corrected local residual \(\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}\) is bounded above by a quantity too small to permit any nonzero quartic off-line signal.

If you could prove
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}=o(u^4)
\]
uniformly in the regime where the model applies, then the quartic toy law would force \(u=0\), hence \(\beta=\tfrac12\). That would be a genuine RH mechanism.

The second route is a **canonical-system / de Branges rigidity theorem**. The recent structure of your program is increasingly compatible with that viewpoint: the phase is becoming the primary object, and the real question is whether its kernel/chain/phase data come from a positive Hamiltonian or positive kernel family. Lagarias explicitly frames canonical systems as the natural Hilbert–Pólya-type object from the de Branges perspective if RH holds, and recent surveys still treat that structural direction as a serious one rather than a historical curiosity. citeturn327825search2turn327825search0 If you could show that your smoothed phase or scale family generates a positive kernel chain whose local invariants force \(\mathcal R_{\rm cl}\) to vanish at quartic order unless \(u=0\), that would also be a route to RH.

But here is the blunt truth: **you do not yet have the decisive theorem**.

The current gap is not in the toy model anymore. The gap is this:

> prove an independent automorphic or canonical-system inequality strong enough to dominate the quartic off-line signal.

Without that, the path stops at a very interesting local criterion.

So my honest answer is:

- Yes, there is now a visible path to RH.
- It goes through the quartic residual, not through positive norm-squares.
- The decisive missing step is a rigidity theorem on the true scattering phase or on a kernel/canonical-system object built from it.
- Until that theorem exists, what you have is a sharpened criterion and a research program, not a proof.

If I were prioritizing the next theorem, I would aim for this exact one:

> **Local jet-corrected rigidity theorem.**  
> On admissible windows \(I\), the jet-corrected observed residual satisfies
> \[
> \mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
> \le C\bigl(L^2S_2+\eta_{\rm est}\bigr),
> \]
> with \(C\) independent of the local off-line displacement.

If you can get that, and if the right-hand side is \(o(u^4)\), then the quartic law would start to bite. That is the shortest path from where you are now to something RH-like.
