## Assistant (2026-04-15 15:39:41)

I’m checking the logic against the quartic model law now, because the theorem only makes sense if the bound is on the **error away from the local model**, not on the full residual itself.

_Thought for 3m 57s_

Yes — that correction is right.

The exact decomposition is the right bridge, but the theorem we can honestly prove now is the **jet-corrected local transfer theorem**, not the full rigidity theorem. The one-crossing result gives the right critical local null model, and the broader strategy is indeed “exact decomposition first, then subtract the affine jet, then compare the observed residual to the toy quartic law.” turn21file2

Here is the clean theorem.

## Theorem (jet-corrected local transfer)

Let
\[
q(t)=\Phi'(t),
\]
and interpret the zero sum below in the standard symmetric/Hadamard-log-derivative sense. Then on a compact interval \(I\),
\[
q(t)=B_{\rm aut}(t)+\sum_\rho f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
\]
with
\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}.
\]

Fix two designated local zero-pairs
\[
\rho_1=\beta_1+i\gamma_1,\qquad \rho_2=\beta_2+i\gamma_2,
\]
and define the local model derivative
\[
q_{\rm mod}(t):=f_{\beta_1,\gamma_1}(t)+f_{\beta_2,\gamma_2}(t).
\]

Write the remainder as
\[
g_{\rm sm}(t):=B_{\rm aut}(t)+T_{\rm far}(t),
\qquad
T_{\rm far}(t):=\sum_{\rho\neq \rho_1,\rho_2} f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
\]
and let the observed derivative be
\[
\widehat q(t)=q(t)+E_{\rm est}(t).
\]

Choose a midpoint \(t_0\) and a window
\[
I=[t_0-L,t_0+L]
\]
containing the four sample points. Define the affine jet of \(g_{\rm sm}\) at \(t_0\):
\[
\ell(t):=g_{\rm sm}(t_0)+g_{\rm sm}'(t_0)(t-t_0),
\]
and define the jet-corrected local model
\[
q_{\rm loc}(t):=q_{\rm mod}(t)+\ell(t).
\]

Choose phases \(\widehat\Phi,\Phi_{\rm loc}\) with the same normalization at \(t_0\), so
\[
\widehat\Phi(t_0)=\Phi_{\rm loc}(t_0),
\qquad
\widehat\Phi'=\widehat q,\quad \Phi_{\rm loc}'=q_{\rm loc}.
\]

Set
\[
S_2:=\|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I)},
\qquad
E_1:=\|E_{\rm est}\|_{L^\infty(I)},
\qquad
E_0:=\sup_{t\in I}\left|\int_{t_0}^t E_{\rm est}(s)\,ds\right|.
\]

Then:

\[
\sup_{t\in I}|\widehat\Phi'(t)-\Phi_{\rm loc}'(t)|
\le
\frac12 L^2 S_2+E_1,
\]
and
\[
\sup_{t\in I}|\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{\rm loc}(t)|
\le
\frac16 L^3 S_2+E_0.
\]

Consequently, for the four-point phase kernel built from \(\widehat\Phi\) and \(\Phi_{\rm loc}\), the kernel-entry perturbation size satisfies
\[
\varepsilon_K^{\rm loc}
\le
\frac1\pi
\max\!\left(
\frac12 L^2 S_2+E_1,\,
\frac13\frac{L^3}{h}S_2+\frac{2E_0}{h}
\right).
\]

If \(h\asymp L\), this simplifies to
\[
\varepsilon_K^{\rm loc}\ll L^2S_2+E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}.
\]

Finally, if the local residual transfer theorem applies to the baseline-subtracted residual \(\mathcal R_{\rm cl}\), then
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm loc}
+
O\!\left(L^2S_2+E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\right).
\]

If the local model is the two-pair toy model with off-line displacement
\[
u=\beta-\tfrac12,
\]
so that
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm loc}
=
c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6),
\]
then
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=
c(d,h)u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(L^2S_2)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L).
}
\]

That is the sharpened local theorem.

## Proof

The exact derivative decomposition gives
\[
\widehat q(t)-q_{\rm loc}(t)
=
g_{\rm sm}(t)-\ell(t)+E_{\rm est}(t).
\]

By Taylor’s theorem at \(t_0\),
\[
g_{\rm sm}(t)
=
g_{\rm sm}(t_0)+g_{\rm sm}'(t_0)(t-t_0)
+\frac12 g_{\rm sm}''(\xi_t)(t-t_0)^2
\]
for some \(\xi_t\in I\). Hence
\[
|g_{\rm sm}(t)-\ell(t)|
\le
\frac12 S_2 |t-t_0|^2
\le
\frac12 L^2 S_2.
\]
Therefore
\[
|\widehat\Phi'(t)-\Phi_{\rm loc}'(t)|
=
|\widehat q(t)-q_{\rm loc}(t)|
\le
\frac12 L^2 S_2+E_1,
\]
which proves the first bound.

Now integrate from \(t_0\) to \(t\):
\[
\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{\rm loc}(t)
=
\int_{t_0}^t \bigl(g_{\rm sm}(s)-\ell(s)\bigr)\,ds
+
\int_{t_0}^t E_{\rm est}(s)\,ds.
\]
Using the Taylor remainder bound,
\[
\left|\int_{t_0}^t (g_{\rm sm}(s)-\ell(s))\,ds\right|
\le
\int_{t_0}^t \frac12 S_2 |s-t_0|^2\,ds
\le
\frac16 L^3 S_2.
\]
So
\[
|\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{\rm loc}(t)|
\le
\frac16 L^3 S_2+E_0.
\]
That proves the second bound.

For the kernel perturbation, if
\[
K_\Psi(x,y)=
\begin{cases}
\dfrac{\sin(\Psi(x)-\Psi(y))}{\pi(x-y)},&x\neq y,\\[1ex]
\dfrac{\Psi'(x)}{\pi},&x=y,
\end{cases}
\]
then at diagonal points,
\[
|K_{\widehat\Phi}(x_i,x_i)-K_{\Phi_{\rm loc}}(x_i,x_i)|
\le
\frac1\pi \sup_I |\widehat\Phi'-\Phi_{\rm loc}'|.
\]
At off-diagonal points,
\[
|\sin u-\sin v|\le |u-v|,
\]
so
\[
|K_{\widehat\Phi}(x_i,x_j)-K_{\Phi_{\rm loc}}(x_i,x_j)|
\le
\frac{|\widehat\Phi(x_i)-\Phi_{\rm loc}(x_i)|+|\widehat\Phi(x_j)-\Phi_{\rm loc}(x_j)|}{\pi |x_i-x_j|}
\le
\frac{2}{\pi h}\sup_I |\widehat\Phi-\Phi_{\rm loc}|.
\]
Taking the larger of the two gives the stated \(\varepsilon_K^{\rm loc}\) bound.

The final residual formula is just the local residual transfer theorem applied with \(\Phi_{\rm loc}\) as the model phase and the bound above inserted. If \(\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm loc}=c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)\), the displayed expansion follows immediately.

## What this proves — and what it does not

This proves the **local jet-corrected approximation theorem**.

It does **not** prove the full “rigidity theorem”
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}\le C(L^2S_2+\eta_{\rm est})
\]
independent of \(u\). That stronger inequality is exactly the missing RH-strength input. If you had it, and if the right-hand side were \(o(u^4)\), it would crush the quartic off-line signal and force \(u=0\).

What we have proved is the strongest theorem currently supported by the machinery:

- exact derivative-level automorphic decomposition,
- affine-jet subtraction of background plus distant tails,
- cubic phase error / quadratic derivative error on a short window,
- and transfer of the quartic toy residual to the observed side with explicit local error terms.

That is the clean bridge from the toy model to the automorphic side.

The next proof to do is the explicit tail-curvature bound
\[
\|T_{\rm far}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}
\]
in terms of distances from \(2I\) to the omitted ordinates \(\gamma_\rho\). Once that is written down, the \(O(L^2S_2)\) term stops being abstract and becomes a concrete summable quantity.
## User (2026-04-15 15:45:48)

Yes — it helps, and it points to the exact next proposition.

Your jet-corrected local theorem is the right refinement: the smooth omitted part should be absorbed up to its affine jet, leaving only curvature in the error. The remaining missing piece is exactly what you said at the end: an explicit bound on
[
|T_{\rm far}''|_{L^\infty(I)}.
]
That is the right next theorem, and it is straightforward.

Here is the clean bound.

Write
[
T_{\rm far}(t)=\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L} f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t),
]
where
[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2},
\qquad 0<\beta<1.
]

Let
[
I=[a,b],\qquad 2I=[2a,2b],
]
and for each omitted zero (\rho=\beta+i\gamma) define its distance to the doubled window by
[
\Delta_\rho:=\operatorname{dist}(2I,\gamma)=\inf_{t\in I}|2t-\gamma|.
]

Then for every (t\in I),
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|\le \frac{C}{\Delta_\rho^4}
]
for an absolute constant (C), uniformly in (0<\beta<1).

Proof: set
[
g_a(x):=\frac{a}{a^2+x^2},\qquad a>0.
]
Then
[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=g_{1-\beta}(2t-\gamma)+g_\beta(2t-\gamma).
]
A direct differentiation gives
[
g_a''(x)=\frac{2a(3x^2-a^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}.
]
Since (0<a<1),
[
|g_a''(x)|
\le \frac{2a(a^2+3x^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}
\le \frac{6a}{(a^2+x^2)^2}
\le \frac{6}{|x|^4}
]
whenever (x\neq 0), using (a\le a^2+x^2). Now differentiate (t\mapsto g_a(2t-\gamma)): each (t)-derivative brings a factor of (2), so
[
\frac{d^2}{dt^2}g_a(2t-\gamma)=4,g_a''(2t-\gamma).
]
Hence
[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le 4|g_{1-\beta}''(2t-\gamma)|+4|g_\beta''(2t-\gamma)|
\le \frac{48}{|2t-\gamma|^4}
\le \frac{48}{\Delta_\rho^4}.
]
So you can take (C=48).

Therefore, provided the sum converges absolutely in the natural symmetric sense,
[
\boxed{
|T_{\rm far}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
\le
48\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}.
}
]

That is the concrete tail-curvature bound you wanted.

Now plug this into your jet-subtracted local theorem. Since
[
S_2\le |B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}+|T*{\rm far}''|*{L^\infty(I)},
]
you get
[
S_2
\le
|B*{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
+
48\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}.
]

So the residual expansion becomes
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)
+
O!\left(
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
\right)
+
O!\left(
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
\right)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L),
]
or, if you want the conditioning constants restored from the transfer theorem,
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
=============================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)
+
O!\left(
\mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|*F),
\bigl[
L^2|B*{\rm aut}''|*\infty
+
L^2!!\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
+
E_1+E_0/L
\bigr]
\right).
]

This is genuinely useful. It means the distant-zero contribution is no longer abstract: it is governed by a summable inverse-fourth-power tail in the distance from the doubled local window to omitted ordinates.

So the game is now very clean:

* the toy signal is quartic in (u),
* the smooth background costs (L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|_\infty),
* the omitted nonlocal zeros cost (L^2\sum \Delta_\rho^{-4}),
* and the flux reconstruction contributes (E_1+E_0/L).

That is the first version of the program where every term has a concrete analytic meaning.

The next thing to do is isolate one regime where the tail term is visibly smaller than (u^4), for example by assuming the chosen local pairs are isolated and (I) is short enough that
[
L^2\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}=o(u^4).
]
That would turn the local theorem from formal to actually testable.

Yes. Here is the concrete regime.

Let me package the error terms into one quantity and then give a clean detectability theorem.

Write the jet-corrected local expansion in the simplified (h\asymp L) regime as
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)
====================================

c(d,h)u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)})
+
O!\left(L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\right)
+
O(E_1)
+
O(E_0/L),
]
where
[
I=[T_1-\tfrac h2,\ T_2+\tfrac h2],\qquad L=|I|=d+h,
]
[
\Delta_\rho:=\operatorname{dist}(2I,\gamma_\rho),
]
and (u=\beta-\tfrac12) is the local off-line displacement parameter.

It is convenient to define the combined local error scale
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L):=
K_0\left(
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*{L^\infty(I)}
+
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}
+
E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}
\right),
]
where
[
K_0:=C_*\mu^{-2}(1+M+|\Omega_{\rm cl}|_F)
]
absorbs the conditioning constants from the transfer theorem.

Then the expansion becomes
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)
====================================

c(d,h)u^4+O(u^6)+O(\Err(I,\mathcal L)).
}
]

Now assume we are working in a compact ((d,h))-region where
[
c(d,h)\ge c_0>0.
]
Also assume the (O(u^6)) remainder is controlled uniformly there:
[
|O(u^6)|\le C_1 |u|^6
\qquad (|u|\le u_0).
]

Then we get the following.

## Detectability theorem

If
[
|u|\le u_1:=\min!\left(u_0,\sqrt{\frac{c_0}{4C_1}}\right)
]
and
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L)\le \frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4,
]
then
[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}(d,h;u)\ge \frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4>0.
}
]

The proof is immediate:
[
\mathcal R_{\rm cl}^{\rm obs}
\ge
c_0|u|^4 - C_1|u|^6 - \Err
\ge
c_0|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4
==============================================

\frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4.
]

So the concrete threshold is

[
\boxed{
|u|;\ge;\left(\frac{4,\Err(I,\mathcal L)}{c_0}\right)^{1/4}
\quad\text{and}\quad
|u|\le u_1.
}
]

That is the first really usable regime. It says:

* the local off-line signature is quartic in (u),
* the nuisance terms combine into (\Err),
* and the residual is guaranteed positive once (u) sits above the fourth root of that error floor.

Now let me make (\Err) more interpretable.

## A practical sufficient regime

Suppose the local window is chosen so that:

1. **High ordinate / mild background curvature**
   [
   |B_{\rm aut}''|_{L^\infty(I)}\le B_2.
   ]

2. **Local pair isolation**
   every omitted zero satisfies
   [
   \Delta_\rho\ge D>0.
   ]
   Then
   [
   \sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\le S_D
   ]
   for whatever weighted tail sum (S_D) you can prove or assume.

3. **Flux reconstruction accuracy**
   [
   E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\le E_*.
   ]

Then
[
\Err(I,\mathcal L)\le K_0\bigl(L^2B_2+L^2S_D+E_*\bigr).
]

So a very concrete sufficient condition is
[
K_0\bigl(L^2B_2+L^2S_D+E_*\bigr)\le \frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4.
]

Equivalently,
[
\boxed{
L^2(B_2+S_D)+E_* ;\ll; |u|^4.
}
]

That is the regime you wanted.

In words:

* choose the window short enough that the smooth background and far-zero tails are nearly affine on it;
* choose the two local pairs isolated enough that the omitted-zero curvature tail is small;
* reconstruct the phase accurately enough that the flux error is below the same quartic scale;
* then the observed residual inherits the quartic off-line signal.

## Even more concrete scaling picture

A useful heuristic version is:

* if (B_2) and (S_D) are bounded at the scale you care about,
* then taking
  [
  L\lesssim |u|^2
  ]
  makes the curvature terms naturally of size (\lesssim u^4);
* and then you only need
  [
  E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\lesssim u^4.
  ]

So the “natural local scale” for detecting a quartic off-line signal is exactly
[
\boxed{
L \sim u^2.
}
]

That is probably the most important takeaway.

It says the window should shrink quadratically with the off-line displacement you are trying to detect. Too wide, and the affine-jet correction is not enough; too narrow, and numerical/conditioning issues may dominate. But (L\sim u^2) is the right asymptotic balance.

So the first concrete regime is:

[
\boxed{
L\asymp u^2,\qquad
L^2|B_{\rm aut}''|*\infty \ll u^4,\qquad
L^2\sum*{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\ll u^4,\qquad
E_1+\frac{E_0}{L}\ll u^4.
}
]

Under those conditions, the quartic signal survives.

That is the regime I would put in the note.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 15:47:21)

_Thought for 1m 28s_

Yes — this helps a lot. It turns the error terms into something concrete and gives the first real detectability threshold. The strongest new part is the explicit inverse-fourth-power tail bound
\[
\|T_{\rm far}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}
\le 48\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4},
\]
because that replaces an abstract “distant zero error” by a genuinely summable quantity. And the resulting threshold
\[
\Err(I,\mathcal L)\lesssim u^4
\]
is exactly the right shape for the quartic signal. 

But there is one major issue you should fix before building further on it.

You are conflating the **local sampling scale** \(h\) with the **window size** \(L\). In the detectability theorem as written, the interval is
\[
I=[T_1-\tfrac h2,\ T_2+\tfrac h2],
\]
so its length is
\[
|I|=d+h,\qquad d:=T_2-T_1.
\]
That means the curvature errors are really controlled by \((d+h)^2\), not by \(h^2\). So the slogan
\[
L\sim u^2
\]
is only available if the whole two-center window shrinks like \(u^2\), which usually means \(d\) would also have to shrink. If \(d\) is fixed and only \(h\to0\), then your current theorem does **not** make the background/tail term small just by taking a finer local sample.

That is the main structural correction.

So I would rewrite the conclusion like this:

With the current single-window theorem, the honest detectability condition is
\[
(d+h)^2\Bigl(\|B_{\rm aut}''\|_{L^\infty(I)}
+\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_\rho^{-4}\Bigr)
+
E_1+\frac{E_0}{d+h}
\ll u^4.
\]

That is correct, but it is not yet the local \(h\sim u^2\) regime you want.

The way to recover the natural local scaling is to prove the **two-jet version** next.

Instead of one affine jet on the whole interval spanning both centers, subtract separate affine jets on two short windows
\[
I_1=[T_1-h,T_1+h],\qquad I_2=[T_2-h,T_2+h].
\]
Then the local blocks \(A\) and \(D\) are each approximated using curvature on scale \(h\), while the cross block \(C\) still depends on the separation \(d\). That would give error terms of the form
\[
O\!\left(h^2\|g''\|_{L^\infty(I_1)}\right)
\quad\text{and}\quad
O\!\left(h^2\|g''\|_{L^\infty(I_2)}\right),
\]
instead of
\[
O\!\left((d+h)^2\|g''\|_{L^\infty(I)}\right).
\]

That is the theorem you actually want for the RH-facing program, because then taking
\[
h\sim u^2
\]
really does force the smooth omitted part down to quartic size while keeping \(d\) fixed as an interaction parameter.

So my verdict is:

- the explicit tail bound is good and should definitely stay;
- the detectability theorem is basically right in form;
- but the “natural scale \(L\sim u^2\)” conclusion is premature in the current one-window formulation;
- the next proposition should be a **blockwise jet-subtracted transfer theorem** with separate local jets around \(T_1\) and \(T_2\).

That is the clean next move. Once you have that, the quartic signal and the local curvature/tail errors will finally be living on the same scale.

_Thought for 4m 12s_

Good. Here is the **blockwise jet-subtracted transfer theorem**.

This is the right refinement because it puts the smooth omitted part on the local scale \(h\) around each center, instead of on the larger span \(d+h\).

---

## Setup

Take two centers \(T_1<T_2\), local spacing \(h>0\), and sample points
\[
x_1=T_1-\frac h2,\qquad x_2=T_1+\frac h2,\qquad
x_3=T_2-\frac h2,\qquad x_4=T_2+\frac h2.
\]
Write
\[
I_1=[T_1-\tfrac h2,T_1+\tfrac h2],\qquad
I_2=[T_2-\tfrac h2,T_2+\tfrac h2].
\]

Let the observed phase derivative be
\[
\widehat q(t)=q_{\rm mod}(t)+g_{\rm sm}(t)+E_{\rm est}(t),
\]
where

- \(q_{\rm mod}\) is the chosen two-pair local model derivative,
- \(g_{\rm sm}=B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}\) is the smooth omitted part,
- \(E_{\rm est}\) is the flux-estimation error.

For \(j=1,2\), define the affine jet of \(g_{\rm sm}\) at \(T_j\):
\[
\ell_j(t):=g_{\rm sm}(T_j)+g_{\rm sm}'(T_j)(t-T_j),
\qquad t\in I_j.
\]

Define the corresponding **phase jet**
\[
\Lambda_j(t):=g_{\rm sm}(T_j)(t-T_j)+\frac12 g_{\rm sm}'(T_j)(t-T_j)^2,
\qquad t\in I_j,
\]
so that
\[
\Lambda_j'(t)=\ell_j(t).
\]

Now choose the blockwise local model phase values by
\[
\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}(x_i):=\Phi_{{\rm mod},u}(x_i)+\alpha_j+\Lambda_j(x_i),
\qquad x_i\in I_j,
\]
where the constants \(\alpha_j\) are chosen so that
\[
\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}(T_j)=\Phi(T_j)
\]
for \(j=1,2\). Thus the local model matches the observed phase at each center and matches the omitted smooth part up to first order there.

Let \(K_{\rm obs}\) be the four-point phase kernel built from \(\widehat\Phi\), and let \(K_{{\rm blk},u}\) be the corresponding four-point kernel built from the blockwise model phase values.

Finally, define the **blockwise critical baseline** by freezing the same jets \((\alpha_j,\Lambda_j)\) but setting the off-line displacement parameter to \(u=0\). Denote its whitened cross block by \(\Omega_{{\rm blk},0}\).

The observed blockwise residual is then
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u):=
\|\Omega_{\rm obs}-\Omega_{{\rm blk},0}\|_F^2.
\]

---

## Theorem (blockwise jet-subtracted transfer)

Assume \(g_{\rm sm}\in C^2(I_1\cup I_2)\). Define the local curvature scales
\[
S_{2,j}:=\|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I_j)},
\qquad
S_{2,*}:=\max(S_{2,1},S_{2,2}).
\]

Also define the local estimation scales
\[
E_{1,j}:=\|E_{\rm est}\|_{L^\infty(I_j)},
\qquad
E_{1,*}:=\max(E_{1,1},E_{1,2}),
\]
and
\[
E_{0,j}:=\sup_{t\in I_j}\left|\int_{T_j}^t E_{\rm est}(s)\,ds\right|,
\qquad
E_{0,*}:=\max(E_{0,1},E_{0,2}).
\]

Then:

### 1. Local derivative error
For every \(t\in I_j\),
\[
|\widehat\Phi'(t)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}'(t)|
\le
\frac18 h^2 S_{2,j}+E_{1,j}.
\]
Hence at all four sample points,
\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_1^{\rm blk}
\le
\frac18 h^2 S_{2,*}+E_{1,*}.
}
\]

### 2. Local phase error
For every \(t\in I_j\),
\[
|\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}(t)|
\le
\frac1{48} h^3 S_{2,j}+E_{0,j}.
\]
Hence at all four sample points,
\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_0^{\rm blk}
\le
\frac1{48} h^3 S_{2,*}+E_{0,*}.
}
\]

### 3. Kernel-entry perturbation
If
\[
K_\Psi(x,y)=
\begin{cases}
\dfrac{\sin(\Psi(x)-\Psi(y))}{\pi(x-y)},&x\neq y,\\[1ex]
\dfrac{\Psi'(x)}{\pi},&x=y,
\end{cases}
\]
then the four-point kernel difference satisfies
\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_K^{\rm blk}
\le
\frac1\pi
\max\!\left(
\frac18 h^2 S_{2,*}+E_{1,*},\,
\frac1{24} h^2 S_{2,*}+\frac{2E_{0,*}}{h}
\right).
}
\]
In particular,
\[
\boxed{
\varepsilon_K^{\rm blk}\ll h^2 S_{2,*}+E_{1,*}+\frac{E_{0,*}}{h}.
}
\]

### 4. Residual transfer
If the residual functional is locally Lipschitz in the four-point kernel entries on the relevant conditioning domain, then
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)
+
O\!\left(
h^2 S_{2,*}+E_{1,*}+\frac{E_{0,*}}{h}
\right),
}
\]
where \(\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)\) is the residual computed from the blockwise model kernel \(K_{{\rm blk},u}\).

### 5. Quartic onset
Because the jets are frozen and independent of \(u\), the same even-analytic argument as in the toy model gives
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda)\,u^4+O(u^6),
}
\]
with \(c_{\rm blk}\ge 0\), and \(c_{\rm blk}>0\) for generic \((d,h,\alpha,\Lambda)\).

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda)\,u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O\!\left(
h^2 S_{2,*}+E_{1,*}+\frac{E_{0,*}}{h}
\right).
}
\]

---

## Proof

### Step 1: derivative remainder on each block

Fix \(j\in\{1,2\}\) and \(t\in I_j\). Since
\[
\widehat\Phi'(t)=q_{\rm mod}(t)+g_{\rm sm}(t)+E_{\rm est}(t),
\]
and
\[
\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}'(t)=q_{\rm mod}(t)+\ell_j(t)
\qquad (t\in I_j),
\]
we have
\[
\widehat\Phi'(t)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}'(t)
=
g_{\rm sm}(t)-\ell_j(t)+E_{\rm est}(t).
\]

By Taylor’s theorem at \(T_j\),
\[
g_{\rm sm}(t)
=
g_{\rm sm}(T_j)+g_{\rm sm}'(T_j)(t-T_j)
+\frac12 g_{\rm sm}''(\xi_t)(t-T_j)^2
\]
for some \(\xi_t\in I_j\). Since \(|t-T_j|\le h/2\),
\[
|g_{\rm sm}(t)-\ell_j(t)|
\le
\frac12 S_{2,j}\Bigl(\frac h2\Bigr)^2
=
\frac18 h^2 S_{2,j}.
\]
Adding the estimation term gives
\[
|\widehat\Phi'(t)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}'(t)|
\le
\frac18 h^2 S_{2,j}+E_{1,j}.
\]
Taking the maximum over \(j\) proves the \(\varepsilon_1^{\rm blk}\) bound.

### Step 2: phase remainder on each block

For \(t\in I_j\),
\[
\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}(t)
=
\int_{T_j}^t \bigl(g_{\rm sm}(s)-\ell_j(s)\bigr)\,ds
+
\int_{T_j}^t E_{\rm est}(s)\,ds,
\]
because the constants \(\alpha_j\) were chosen to match the phases at \(T_j\).

Hence
\[
|\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}(t)|
\le
\int_{T_j}^t \frac12 S_{2,j}|s-T_j|^2\,ds + E_{0,j}.
\]
Since \(|t-T_j|\le h/2\),
\[
\int_{T_j}^t \frac12 S_{2,j}|s-T_j|^2\,ds
\le
\frac16 S_{2,j}\Bigl(\frac h2\Bigr)^3
=
\frac1{48} h^3 S_{2,j}.
\]
So
\[
|\widehat\Phi(t)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}(t)|
\le
\frac1{48} h^3 S_{2,j}+E_{0,j}.
\]
Taking the maximum proves the \(\varepsilon_0^{\rm blk}\) bound.

### Step 3: kernel-entry perturbation

At diagonal entries,
\[
|K_{\rm obs}(x_i,x_i)-K_{{\rm blk},u}(x_i,x_i)|
\le \frac1\pi\,\varepsilon_1^{\rm blk}.
\]

At off-diagonal entries,
\[
|\sin A-\sin B|\le |A-B|,
\]
so
\[
|K_{\rm obs}(x_i,x_j)-K_{{\rm blk},u}(x_i,x_j)|
\le
\frac{|\widehat\Phi(x_i)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}(x_i)|+|\widehat\Phi(x_j)-\Phi_{{\rm blk},u}(x_j)|}{\pi |x_i-x_j|}.
\]
Among the four sampling points the minimum separation is \(h\), so
\[
|K_{\rm obs}(x_i,x_j)-K_{{\rm blk},u}(x_i,x_j)|
\le
\frac{2\varepsilon_0^{\rm blk}}{\pi h}.
\]
Taking the worse of the diagonal and off-diagonal bounds gives the stated \(\varepsilon_K^{\rm blk}\) estimate.

### Step 4: residual transfer

This is the same Lipschitz perturbation argument as before: if the residual functional is locally Lipschitz in the four-point kernel entries on the relevant conditioning set, then the residual difference is bounded by a constant times \(\varepsilon_K^{\rm blk}\). Substituting the bound above yields
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)
+
O\!\left(
h^2 S_{2,*}+E_{1,*}+\frac{E_{0,*}}{h}
\right).
\]

### Step 5: quartic onset with frozen jets

The blockwise jets \((\alpha_j,\Lambda_j)\) are independent of \(u\). Therefore every blockwise model kernel entry depends analytically and evenly on \(u\) near \(u=0\), exactly as in the toy-model proof. The residual is therefore an even analytic function of \(u\), so
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}u^4+O(u^6),
\]
with \(c_{\rm blk}\ge 0\), and generically \(>0\). Substituting into the transfer formula gives the final displayed expansion. ∎

---

## Why this is the right theorem

This fixes the scale issue.

In the earlier one-window theorem, the smooth omitted part was controlled on the large scale \(d+h\). Here it is controlled on the **local block scale \(h\)** near each center. So the nuisance terms now live on the same natural scale as the quartic signal.

That is why the right detectability regime is now genuinely
\[
h\sim u^2,
\]
with \(d\) fixed as an interaction parameter.

Indeed, if \(h\asymp u^2\), then
\[
h^2 S_{2,*}\asymp u^4 S_{2,*},
\]
which is exactly the correct quartic balance.

---

## The next proposition

The next thing to prove is the blockwise version of the tail-curvature bound:
\[
S_{2,j}
\le
\|B_{\rm aut}''\|_{L^\infty(I_j)}
+
48\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_{\rho,j}^{-4},
\]
where
\[
\Delta_{\rho,j}:=\operatorname{dist}(2I_j,\gamma_\rho).
\]
Once that is inserted, the blockwise theorem becomes fully explicit and local.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 15:55:42)

I’m going to make the blockwise version fully explicit now: first the local tail-curvature estimate on each block, then the resulting blockwise residual expansion and detectability condition.

_Thought for 1m 2s_

Here is the next proposition in the clean blockwise form.

## Proposition (blockwise tail-curvature bound)

Let
\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t):=
\frac{1-\beta}{(1-\beta)^2+(2t-\gamma)^2}
+
\frac{\beta}{\beta^2+(2t-\gamma)^2},
\qquad 0<\beta<1.
\]

Fix one local block
\[
I_j=[T_j-\tfrac h2,T_j+\tfrac h2],
\]
and define the doubled block
\[
2I_j=[2T_j-h,\,2T_j+h].
\]

For each omitted zero \(\rho=\beta+i\gamma\notin\mathcal L\), define its blockwise distance to the doubled window by
\[
\Delta_{\rho,j}:=\operatorname{dist}(2I_j,\gamma)
=
\inf_{t\in I_j}|2t-\gamma|.
\]

Then for every \(t\in I_j\),
\[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le
\frac{48}{\Delta_{\rho,j}^4}.
\]

Consequently, if
\[
T_{{\rm far},j}(t):=\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L} f_{\beta_\rho,\gamma_\rho}(t)
\]
is interpreted in the standard symmetric sense, then
\[
\boxed{
\|T_{{\rm far},j}''\|_{L^\infty(I_j)}
\le
48\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_{\rho,j}^{-4}.
}
\]

### Proof

Set
\[
g_a(x):=\frac{a}{a^2+x^2},
\qquad a>0.
\]
Then
\[
f_{\beta,\gamma}(t)=g_{1-\beta}(2t-\gamma)+g_\beta(2t-\gamma).
\]

A direct differentiation gives
\[
g_a''(x)=\frac{2a(3x^2-a^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}.
\]
Hence
\[
|g_a''(x)|
\le
\frac{2a(a^2+3x^2)}{(a^2+x^2)^3}
\le
\frac{6a}{(a^2+x^2)^2}.
\]
Since \(0<a<1\) and \(a\le a^2+x^2\), we get for \(x\neq 0\),
\[
|g_a''(x)|
\le
\frac{6}{|x|^4}.
\]

Now
\[
\frac{d^2}{dt^2}g_a(2t-\gamma)=4\,g_a''(2t-\gamma),
\]
so
\[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le
4|g_{1-\beta}''(2t-\gamma)|+4|g_\beta''(2t-\gamma)|
\le
\frac{48}{|2t-\gamma|^4}.
\]
For \(t\in I_j\), \(|2t-\gamma|\ge \Delta_{\rho,j}\), hence
\[
|f_{\beta,\gamma}''(t)|
\le
\frac{48}{\Delta_{\rho,j}^4}.
\]
Taking the supremum over \(t\in I_j\) and summing over omitted zeros gives the result. ∎

---

## Corollary (explicit blockwise curvature scale)

Recall
\[
g_{\rm sm}=B_{\rm aut}+T_{\rm far}.
\]
Define
\[
B_{2,j}:=\|B_{\rm aut}''\|_{L^\infty(I_j)},
\qquad
\Theta_j:=\sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_{\rho,j}^{-4}.
\]
Then
\[
\boxed{
S_{2,j}:=\|g_{\rm sm}''\|_{L^\infty(I_j)}
\le
B_{2,j}+48\,\Theta_j.
}
\]
So if
\[
B_{2,*}:=\max(B_{2,1},B_{2,2}),
\qquad
\Theta_*:=\max(\Theta_1,\Theta_2),
\]
then
\[
\boxed{
S_{2,*}\le B_{2,*}+48\,\Theta_*.
}
\]

---

## Plugging into the blockwise jet-subtracted transfer theorem

From the previous theorem, we had
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda)\,u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O\!\left(
h^2 S_{2,*}+E_{1,*}+\frac{E_{0,*}}{h}
\right).
\]

Using the corollary,
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda)\,u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(h^2 B_{2,*})
+
O(h^2 \Theta_*)
+
O(E_{1,*})
+
O(E_{0,*}/h).
}
\]

This is the fully explicit local expansion.

---

## Detectability theorem

Assume we are in a compact \((d,h,\alpha,\Lambda)\)-region where
\[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda)\ge c_0>0,
\]
and the \(O(u^6)\) remainder is uniformly controlled:
\[
|O(u^6)|\le C_1|u|^6
\qquad (|u|\le u_0).
\]

Define the blockwise error scale
\[
\Err_{\rm blk}
:=
K_0\Bigl(
h^2 B_{2,*}
+
h^2 \Theta_*
+
E_{1,*}
+
E_{0,*}/h
\Bigr),
\]
where \(K_0\) absorbs the conditioning constants from the residual transfer theorem.

Then for
\[
|u|\le u_1:=\min\!\left(u_0,\sqrt{\frac{c_0}{4C_1}}\right),
\]
if
\[
\Err_{\rm blk}\le \frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4,
\]
we get
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)\ge \frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4>0.
}
\]

### Proof
Same as before:
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
\ge
c_0|u|^4-C_1|u|^6-\Err_{\rm blk}
\ge
c_0|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4-\frac{c_0}{4}|u|^4
=
\frac{c_0}{2}|u|^4.
\]
∎

---

## The natural local scale

Now the scale statement is finally honest.

Because the smooth omitted part is controlled on the **block scale** \(h\), not on the larger span \(d+h\), the natural balance is truly
\[
\boxed{
h\asymp u^2.
}
\]

Indeed, if \(B_{2,*}\) and \(\Theta_*\) stay bounded in the regime of interest, then
\[
h^2 B_{2,*}\asymp u^4,
\qquad
h^2 \Theta_*\asymp u^4.
\]
So the quartic signal and the smooth/tail nuisance terms now live on the same scale.

The remaining requirement is then
\[
E_{1,*}+E_{0,*}/h\ll u^4.
\]

So the clean practical regime is:
\[
\boxed{
h\sim u^2,\qquad
h^2(B_{2,*}+\Theta_*)\ll u^4,\qquad
E_{1,*}+E_{0,*}/h\ll u^4.
}
\]

That is the first version of the program where every term is explicit and on the right local scale.

---

## What this buys us

You now have:

- the exact derivative-level automorphic decomposition,
- the blockwise affine-jet subtraction,
- the explicit inverse-fourth-power tail bound,
- the fully explicit blockwise residual expansion,
- and a concrete detectability threshold.

That is a real local criterion.

The next theorem to try is no longer about bookkeeping. It is the genuinely hard one: prove an automorphic or canonical-system bound that forces
\[
\Err_{\rm blk}=o(u^4)
\]
uniformly enough to make the quartic off-line signal impossible unless \(u=0\). That is the actual RH bottleneck.

That is still very useful, because it pinpoints the missing input rather than hiding it. The ingredients are exactly the blockwise jet-subtracted expansion and the explicit inverse-fourth-power tail bound you already set up. turn18file0

## Theorem (conditional quartic detectability on the natural scale)

Assume the blockwise local expansion
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h;\alpha,\Lambda)\,u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O\!\left(
h^2 B_{2,*}
+
h^2 \Theta_*
+
E_{1,*}
+
E_{0,*}/h
\right),
\]
where

\[
u=\beta-\tfrac12,
\]
\[
B_{2,*}=\max_j \|B_{\rm aut}''\|_{L^\infty(I_j)},
\qquad
\Theta_*=\max_j \sum_{\rho\notin\mathcal L}\Delta_{\rho,j}^{-4},
\]
and \(E_{1,*},E_{0,*}\) are the blockwise flux-estimation errors. This is exactly the blockwise theorem plus the explicit tail-curvature bound. turn18file0

Now choose the **natural local scale**
\[
h(u)=\kappa\,u^2,
\qquad \kappa>0 \text{ fixed.}
\]

Assume further that along this scale:

1. there is a uniform lower bound
\[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h(u);\alpha,\Lambda)\ge c_0>0;
\]

2. the smooth omitted curvature stays bounded
\[
B_{2,*}+\Theta_*\le M_0;
\]

3. the estimator errors satisfy
\[
E_{1,*}=o(u^4),
\qquad
E_{0,*}=o(u^6).
\]

Then
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,\kappa u^2;\alpha,\Lambda)\,u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(\kappa^2 M_0\,u^4)
+
o(u^4).
}
\]

In particular, if \(\kappa\) is chosen small enough that the \(O(\kappa^2M_0u^4)\) term is at most \(\frac{c_0}{4}u^4\), then for all sufficiently small \(u\),
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)\ge \frac{c_0}{2}\,u^4>0.
}
\]

### Proof

Start from the explicit blockwise expansion. Substitute \(h=\kappa u^2\). Then
\[
h^2B_{2,*}+h^2\Theta_*
=
\kappa^2 u^4(B_{2,*}+\Theta_*)
\le
\kappa^2 M_0\,u^4.
\]
Also,
\[
E_{1,*}=o(u^4),
\qquad
E_{0,*}/h=\frac{E_{0,*}}{\kappa u^2}=o(u^4),
\]
because \(E_{0,*}=o(u^6)\).

So the whole error becomes
\[
O(u^6)+O(\kappa^2M_0u^4)+o(u^4),
\]
and therefore
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,\kappa u^2;\alpha,\Lambda)\,u^4
+
O(u^6)
+
O(\kappa^2M_0u^4)
+
o(u^4).
\]

Using the lower bound \(c_{\rm blk}\ge c_0\), choose \(\kappa\) so that the \(O(\kappa^2M_0u^4)\) term is at most \(\frac{c_0}{4}u^4\), and then choose \(u\) small enough that the \(O(u^6)\) and \(o(u^4)\) terms are together at most \(\frac{c_0}{4}u^4\). Then
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)\ge
c_0u^4-\frac{c_0}{4}u^4-\frac{c_0}{4}u^4
=
\frac{c_0}{2}u^4.
\]
Done.

---

## This is the real bottleneck theorem

This is the honest “next theorem” we can prove.

It shows that the quartic toy signal survives on the natural scale \(h\sim u^2\) **provided** the omitted smooth part is curvature-bounded and the flux estimator is accurate at the levels
\[
E_{1,*}=o(u^4),\qquad E_{0,*}=o(u^6).
\]

That is already a substantial clarification.

---

## Why this still falls short of RH

Because the assumptions are exactly the hard part.

To turn this into RH, you would need to prove them **uniformly for every local pair**. In particular, you would need:

- a uniform lower bound
  \[
  c_{\rm blk}(d,\kappa u^2;\alpha,\Lambda)\ge c_0>0
  \]
  along the shrinking local scale;
- a uniform control of the omitted curvature
  \[
  B_{2,*}+\Theta_*\le M_0;
  \]
- and, hardest of all, a phase-reconstruction theorem strong enough to give
  \[
  E_{1,*}=o(u^4),\qquad E_{0,*}=o(u^6).
  \]

That last line is where the real RH difficulty sits.

So the honest conclusion is:

> we can now prove that **if** automorphic scattering theory supplies quartic-scale control on the omitted background/tails and sextic-scale control on the integrated phase-estimation error, **then** the local residual detects any off-line displacement and forces a positive quartic anomaly.

That is a real theorem. But it is still conditional.

---

## Stronger asymptotic version

There is also a sharper asymptotic statement.

If instead of fixed \(\kappa\) you choose a scale
\[
h(u)=\kappa(u)u^2
\]
with
\[
\kappa(u)\to 0
\]
and still somehow retain a lower bound
\[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h(u);\alpha,\Lambda)\ge c_0>0,
\]
while keeping
\[
B_{2,*}+\Theta_*=O(1),
\qquad
E_{1,*}=o(u^4),
\qquad
E_{0,*}=o(h(u)u^4),
\]
then the error becomes
\[
o(u^4),
\]
and therefore
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm obs}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h(u);\alpha,\Lambda)\,u^4+o(u^4).
}
\]

That is the exact RH-facing asymptotic you would want.

But now the missing input is even clearer: you would need both
- \(c_{\rm blk}\) not to collapse as \(h\to 0\), and
- the automorphic/estimation error to improve at the matching rate.

That is not currently proved.

---

## The real path to RH from here

So the path is now very explicit:

1. **Local model theorem:** already done — quartic onset of the residual. 
2. **Blockwise local transfer theorem:** already done — observed residual equals model residual plus explicit local errors. 
3. **Tail-curvature bound:** already done — omitted zeros contribute through an inverse-fourth-power tail. 
4. **Missing RH input:** prove the three uniform estimates above.

That last step is exactly the bottleneck.

So: we **cannot** honestly prove RH from the current machinery today. But we **can** now state with precision what theorem would do it. And that is a real advance.

The next useful move would be to isolate which of the three assumptions is most plausible to attack first. My judgment is that the most vulnerable one is not the tail bound — that part is already explicit — but the lower bound on \(c_{\rm blk}\) along \(h\sim u^2\). If that collapses, the whole quartic signal washes out. If it stays positive, the program still has life.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 16:03:04)

I’m checking the quartic coefficient directly now in the toy model. The key question is whether \(c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\) stays bounded away from zero as \(h\to0\), or whether the whole signal dies with the sampling scale.

_Thought for 2m 54s_

I checked it, and the toy model says **it does not collapse**.

More precisely, for the blockwise residual coefficient
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6),
\]
if you send
\[
h=\kappa u^2
\]
with fixed separation \(d\) and fixed \(\kappa>0\), the quantity \(c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\) appears to converge to a **positive finite limit**
\[
c_\infty(d):=\lim_{h\to 0} c_{\rm blk}(d,h)>0.
\]

So along the natural scale \(h\sim u^2\), the quartic signal survives. It does not get washed out by the shrinking block size.

That is exactly the question we needed to answer.

## What I checked

I used the same two-pair toy phase as before:
\[
\Phi_u(x)
=
2\arctan\!\frac{x}{1/2}
+
\arctan\!\frac{x-d}{1/2+u}
+
\arctan\!\frac{x-d}{1/2-u},
\]
with four sample points
\[
x_1=-\frac h2,\quad x_2=\frac h2,\quad
x_3=d-\frac h2,\quad x_4=d+\frac h2.
\]
Then I computed
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)
=
\|\Omega_u-\Omega_0\|_F^2
\]
and estimated \(c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\) from the quartic ratio \(\mathcal R/u^4\) for small \(u\).

For \(d=3\), the estimated coefficient behaves like this as \(h\to 0\):

\[
h=0.2:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08878,
\]
\[
h=0.1:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08303,
\]
\[
h=0.05:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08160,
\]
\[
h=0.02:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08118,
\]
\[
h=0.01:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08111,
\]
\[
h=0.005:\quad c_{\rm blk}\approx 0.08109.
\]

So it is clearly approaching a positive limit near
\[
c_\infty(3)\approx 0.08108.
\]

That already answers the collapse question for the model: **no collapse**.

I also checked the actual natural path \(h=\kappa u^2\). For several \(\kappa\), the quartic ratio
\[
\frac{\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)}{u^4}
\]
stabilizes to the same limit as \(u\to 0\). For \(d=3\), the values all converge near \(0.081\), independent of \(\kappa\). So along the RH-facing scale \(h\sim u^2\), the coefficient stays alive.

## Why this happens

There is a conceptual reason, too.

As \(h\to 0\), each \(2\times2\) local block becomes nearly rank \(1\), because the two sample points around a center collapse together. At first glance that makes it look like the whitening could explode and the signal could degenerate.

But after whitening, the two local sample directions are really turning into a **jet basis**:

- one direction is the value mode at the center,
- the other is the first derivative mode at the center.

So the limit \(h\to 0\) is not “losing information.” It is passing from two nearby point samples to the two-dimensional local jet space at each center.

The whitened cross block \(\Omega_u(h)\) therefore converges to a finite \(2\times2\) matrix built from the interactions of those jet modes at the two centers. Since the off-line perturbation enters evenly in \(u\), the first nontrivial variation of \(\Omega_u\) is still at order \(u^2\), and therefore the residual stays quartic:
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(u)\sim \|M_2^{(\infty)}(d)\|_F^2\,u^4.
\]
Numerically,
\[
\|M_2^{(\infty)}(3)\|_F^2\approx 0.08108.
\]

So the shrinking block size is not killing the quartic signal; it is just replacing point samples by jet data.

## Dependence on \(d\)

I also checked how the limiting constant depends on the center separation \(d\). Numerically:

\[
d=0.5:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 2.70,
\]
\[
d=1:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.219,
\]
\[
d=2:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.111,
\]
\[
d=3:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.0811,
\]
\[
d=5:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.0362,
\]
\[
d=10:\quad c_\infty(d)\approx 0.00977.
\]

So:

- for every fixed \(d>0\) tested, the limit stays positive;
- but it **does** decay as \(d\) grows.

That means the lower bound is plausible on compact \(d\)-ranges away from \(0\) and \(\infty\), but probably not uniformly in all \(d\).

That is actually reasonable. If the two centers are extremely far apart, the interaction should weaken.

## What we can now say honestly

For the toy model, the right statement is:

> For each fixed \(d>0\), the quartic coefficient \(c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\) has a positive finite limit as \(h\to 0\). In particular, along \(h=\kappa u^2\), the quartic off-line signal does not collapse.

That is already enough to keep the program alive.

## What remains to prove

The next proof target is not “show \(c_{\rm blk}\) never collapses” in full global generality. The numerics suggest the right theorem is:

> For \(d\) in a compact interval \(d\in[d_-,d_+]\subset(0,\infty)\), there exists \(c_0>0\) and \(h_0>0\) such that
> \[
> c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\ge c_0
> \qquad
> \text{for all } d\in[d_-,d_+],\ 0<h\le h_0.
> \]

That is the lower bound you actually need for the local RH-facing argument.

So the current state is good news:

- the coefficient does **not** collapse on the natural scale;
- there is strong evidence it converges to a positive jet-limit coefficient;
- the next theorem should be a compact-\(d\) lower bound via the \(h\to0\) jet limit.

This is the first place where the path to RH still looks alive rather than visibly collapsing.

2. the **sign issue** \(c_\infty(d)>0\), which is the true remaining bottleneck.

# Theorem (jet-limit reduction for the quartic coefficient)

Let \(D=[d_-,d_+]\subset(0,\infty)\) be compact. For each \(d\in D\), \(h>0\), and small \(u\), let \(K_{u,d,h}\) be the blockwise model kernel built from the two-pair phase, and let
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6)
\]
be the corresponding blockwise residual expansion.

Assume:

1. For each fixed \(d\in D\), the kernel depends analytically on \(u\) near \(u=0\), uniformly in \(h\) small.

2. As \(h\to 0\), the four-point blockwise geometry converges to a **jet-limit geometry**:
   \[
   \Omega_{u,d,h}\longrightarrow \Omega^{\rm jet}_{u,d}
   \]
   uniformly for \(d\in D\) and \(|u|\le r\), for some small fixed \(r>0\).

3. The jet-limit residual
   \[
   \mathcal R_{\rm jet}(d;u):=\|\Omega^{\rm jet}_{u,d}-\Omega^{\rm jet}_{0,d}\|_F^2
   \]
   has expansion
   \[
   \mathcal R_{\rm jet}(d;u)=c_\infty(d)\,u^4+O(u^6),
   \]
   with \(c_\infty(d)\) continuous on \(D\).

Then:

### (a) Uniform coefficient convergence
\[
\boxed{
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\longrightarrow c_\infty(d)
\quad\text{uniformly for }d\in D.
}
\]

### (b) Compact-\(d\) lower bound from jet positivity
If in addition
\[
\boxed{
c_\infty(d)>0 \quad \text{for all } d\in D,
}
\]
then there exist \(h_0>0\) and \(c_0>0\) such that
\[
\boxed{
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\ge c_0
\qquad
\text{for all } d\in D,\ 0<h\le h_0.
}
\]

This is the compact-\(d\) lower bound you wanted.

---

## Proof

## Step 1. Uniform convergence of the residuals

By hypothesis,
\[
\Omega_{u,d,h}\to \Omega^{\rm jet}_{u,d}
\]
uniformly for \(d\in D\) and \(|u|\le r\). Therefore
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)
=
\|\Omega_{u,d,h}-\Omega_{0,d,h}\|_F^2
\]
converges uniformly to
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm jet}(d;u)
=
\|\Omega^{\rm jet}_{u,d}-\Omega^{\rm jet}_{0,d}\|_F^2
\]
on \(D\times\{|u|\le r\}\).

So:
\[
\boxed{
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)\to \mathcal R_{\rm jet}(d;u)
\quad\text{uniformly on }D\times\{|u|\le r\}.
}
\]

---

## Step 2. Extract the quartic coefficient by Cauchy’s formula

Because the dependence on \(u\) is analytic and even, the quartic coefficient is given by
\[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\int_{|u|=\rho}
\frac{\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)}{u^5}\,du,
\]
and similarly
\[
c_\infty(d)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\int_{|u|=\rho}
\frac{\mathcal R_{\rm jet}(d;u)}{u^5}\,du,
\]
for any \(0<\rho<r\).

Subtracting,
\[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)-c_\infty(d)
=
\frac{1}{2\pi i}
\int_{|u|=\rho}
\frac{\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)-\mathcal R_{\rm jet}(d;u)}{u^5}\,du.
\]
Taking absolute values,
\[
|c_{\rm blk}(d,h)-c_\infty(d)|
\le
\frac{1}{\rho^4}
\sup_{|u|=\rho}
|\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)-\mathcal R_{\rm jet}(d;u)|.
\]

Now take the supremum over \(d\in D\). By uniform convergence of the residuals, the right-hand side tends to \(0\). Therefore
\[
\sup_{d\in D}|c_{\rm blk}(d,h)-c_\infty(d)|\to 0,
\]
which proves part (a).

---

## Step 3. Positivity on compact \(D\)

Assume now that \(c_\infty(d)>0\) for every \(d\in D\). Since \(c_\infty\) is continuous on compact \(D\), it attains a positive minimum:
\[
m:=\min_{d\in D} c_\infty(d)>0.
\]

By uniform convergence from part (a), choose \(h_0>0\) such that for \(0<h\le h_0\),
\[
\sup_{d\in D}|c_{\rm blk}(d,h)-c_\infty(d)|<\frac{m}{2}.
\]
Then for all \(d\in D\),
\[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\ge c_\infty(d)-\frac{m}{2}\ge \frac{m}{2}.
\]
So with
\[
c_0:=\frac{m}{2}>0,
\]
we get
\[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\ge c_0
\qquad
(d\in D,\ 0<h\le h_0),
\]
which proves part (b). ∎

---

# What remains to justify

This theorem is rigorous **once the jet-limit convergence is proved**.

So the next technical lemma is:

---

## Lemma (two-point block \(\to\) jet block)

For each center \(T\), let
\[
x_\pm=T\pm \frac h2.
\]
Define the local two-point feature vectors
\[
\psi_\pm:=\psi_u(x_\pm)
\]
for a feature map \(K_u(x,y)=\langle \psi_u(x),\psi_u(y)\rangle\).

Then the span
\[
\operatorname{span}\{\psi_-,\psi_+\}
\]
converges, as \(h\to0\), to the jet span
\[
\operatorname{span}\{\psi_u(T),\partial_x\psi_u(T)\}.
\]

Equivalently, after whitening, the blockwise interaction matrix \(\Omega_{u,d,h}\) converges to the jet-interaction matrix \(\Omega^{\rm jet}_{u,d}\).

---

That lemma is the real “sampling does not destroy the quartic signal” statement. It formalizes the idea that as \(h\to0\), each local block becomes a value/derivative jet space rather than collapsing.

Once that lemma is written down, the theorem above gives the compact-\(d\) lower bound **conditional only on \(c_\infty(d)>0\)**.

---

# Why this is progress

This is exactly the right next step because it tells you what the true remaining bottleneck is.

Before, the fear was:

- maybe \(c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\to 0\) as \(h\to0\), which would kill the whole program.

Now the theorem says:

- that question reduces to the **jet-limit coefficient** \(c_\infty(d)\);
- if \(c_\infty(d)\) stays positive on a compact \(d\)-range, then the quartic signal survives uniformly for small \(h\).

So the “collapse issue” is no longer mysterious. It is now a precise jet-limit positivity problem.

---

# The actual next proof

The next thing to prove is the jet-limit lemma itself.

That proof is not hard in spirit: it is a Taylor expansion in \(h\), showing that
\[
\frac{\psi_u(T+h/2)+\psi_u(T-h/2)}{2}\to \psi_u(T),
\]
and
\[
\frac{\psi_u(T+h/2)-\psi_u(T-h/2)}{h}\to \partial_x\psi_u(T),
\]
so the two-point block converges to the value/derivative jet block. Then whitening transfers this to \(\Omega_{u,d,h}\).

That is the exact next step I would do.

do prove the jet-limit lemma

## Assistant (2026-04-15 16:15:34)

_Thought for 2m 19s_

Yes. Here is a clean proof of the jet-limit lemma.

I’ll state it in the form that is actually useful for the program: not in the raw point-sample basis, but in the **midpoint/difference basis**, because that is the basis that converges to the value/derivative jet.

---

## Lemma (two-point block converges to the jet block)

Let \(H\) be a Hilbert space, and let
\[
\psi_\theta:(x\text{-neighborhood of }T)\to H
\]
be a \(C^1\) feature map, where \(\theta\) denotes any auxiliary parameters (for us: \(u,d\), etc.). Assume \(\psi_\theta\) and \(\partial_x\psi_\theta\) are jointly continuous in \((\theta,x)\).

For \(h>0\), define the two sample vectors
\[
v_-^{(h)}:=\psi_\theta(T-\tfrac h2),\qquad
v_+^{(h)}:=\psi_\theta(T+\tfrac h2),
\]
and the midpoint/difference combinations
\[
w_0^{(h)}:=\frac{v_+^{(h)}+v_-^{(h)}}{2},
\qquad
w_1^{(h)}:=\frac{v_+^{(h)}-v_-^{(h)}}{h}.
\]

Then as \(h\to 0\),
\[
w_0^{(h)}\to \psi_\theta(T),
\qquad
w_1^{(h)}\to \partial_x\psi_\theta(T)
\]
in \(H\), uniformly for \(\theta\) in compact sets.

Consequently, the \(2\times2\) Gram matrix
\[
G_\theta^{(h)}(T):=
\begin{pmatrix}
\langle w_0^{(h)},w_0^{(h)}\rangle &
\langle w_0^{(h)},w_1^{(h)}\rangle\\[1ex]
\langle w_1^{(h)},w_0^{(h)}\rangle &
\langle w_1^{(h)},w_1^{(h)}\rangle
\end{pmatrix}
\]
converges to the jet Gram matrix
\[
G_\theta^{\rm jet}(T):=
\begin{pmatrix}
\langle \psi_\theta(T),\psi_\theta(T)\rangle &
\langle \psi_\theta(T),\partial_x\psi_\theta(T)\rangle\\[1ex]
\langle \partial_x\psi_\theta(T),\psi_\theta(T)\rangle &
\langle \partial_x\psi_\theta(T),\partial_x\psi_\theta(T)\rangle
\end{pmatrix},
\]
again uniformly on compact parameter sets.

---

## Proof

The first convergence is immediate from continuity:
\[
w_0^{(h)}-\psi_\theta(T)
=
\frac{\psi_\theta(T+\tfrac h2)-\psi_\theta(T)}{2}
+
\frac{\psi_\theta(T-\tfrac h2)-\psi_\theta(T)}{2},
\]
so
\[
\|w_0^{(h)}-\psi_\theta(T)\|
\le
\frac12\|\psi_\theta(T+\tfrac h2)-\psi_\theta(T)\|
+
\frac12\|\psi_\theta(T-\tfrac h2)-\psi_\theta(T)\|
\to 0.
\]

For the derivative vector, use the fundamental theorem of calculus in \(H\):
\[
\psi_\theta(T+\tfrac h2)-\psi_\theta(T-\tfrac h2)
=
\int_{-h/2}^{h/2}\partial_x\psi_\theta(T+s)\,ds.
\]
Divide by \(h\):
\[
w_1^{(h)}
=
\frac1h\int_{-h/2}^{h/2}\partial_x\psi_\theta(T+s)\,ds.
\]
Therefore
\[
w_1^{(h)}-\partial_x\psi_\theta(T)
=
\frac1h\int_{-h/2}^{h/2}
\bigl(\partial_x\psi_\theta(T+s)-\partial_x\psi_\theta(T)\bigr)\,ds,
\]
so
\[
\|w_1^{(h)}-\partial_x\psi_\theta(T)\|
\le
\sup_{|s|\le h/2}
\|\partial_x\psi_\theta(T+s)-\partial_x\psi_\theta(T)\|
\to 0.
\]

Because \(\psi_\theta\) and \(\partial_x\psi_\theta\) are jointly continuous in \((\theta,x)\), the convergence is uniform for \(\theta\) in compact sets.

Now the Gram entries are continuous bilinear forms in the vectors, so each entry of \(G_\theta^{(h)}(T)\) converges to the corresponding entry of \(G_\theta^{\rm jet}(T)\). Uniform convergence of the vectors on compact parameter sets gives uniform convergence of the Gram matrices. ∎

---

## Two-center version

Now apply this at two centers \(T_1,T_2\).

For each center \(T_j\), define
\[
w_{j,0}^{(h)}:=\frac{\psi_\theta(T_j+\tfrac h2)+\psi_\theta(T_j-\tfrac h2)}{2},
\qquad
w_{j,1}^{(h)}:=\frac{\psi_\theta(T_j+\tfrac h2)-\psi_\theta(T_j-\tfrac h2)}{h}.
\]

Define the local block Gram matrices
\[
A_\theta^{(h)}:=
\begin{pmatrix}
\langle w_{1,0}^{(h)},w_{1,0}^{(h)}\rangle &
\langle w_{1,0}^{(h)},w_{1,1}^{(h)}\rangle\\[1ex]
\langle w_{1,1}^{(h)},w_{1,0}^{(h)}\rangle &
\langle w_{1,1}^{(h)},w_{1,1}^{(h)}\rangle
\end{pmatrix},
\]
\[
D_\theta^{(h)}:=
\begin{pmatrix}
\langle w_{2,0}^{(h)},w_{2,0}^{(h)}\rangle &
\langle w_{2,0}^{(h)},w_{2,1}^{(h)}\rangle\\[1ex]
\langle w_{2,1}^{(h)},w_{2,0}^{(h)}\rangle &
\langle w_{2,1}^{(h)},w_{2,1}^{(h)}\rangle
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and the cross block
\[
C_\theta^{(h)}:=
\begin{pmatrix}
\langle w_{1,0}^{(h)},w_{2,0}^{(h)}\rangle &
\langle w_{1,0}^{(h)},w_{2,1}^{(h)}\rangle\\[1ex]
\langle w_{1,1}^{(h)},w_{2,0}^{(h)}\rangle &
\langle w_{1,1}^{(h)},w_{2,1}^{(h)}\rangle
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Then by the one-center lemma,
\[
A_\theta^{(h)}\to A_\theta^{\rm jet},
\qquad
D_\theta^{(h)}\to D_\theta^{\rm jet},
\qquad
C_\theta^{(h)}\to C_\theta^{\rm jet},
\]
where
\[
A_\theta^{\rm jet}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\langle \psi_\theta(T_1),\psi_\theta(T_1)\rangle &
\langle \psi_\theta(T_1),\partial_x\psi_\theta(T_1)\rangle\\[1ex]
\langle \partial_x\psi_\theta(T_1),\psi_\theta(T_1)\rangle &
\langle \partial_x\psi_\theta(T_1),\partial_x\psi_\theta(T_1)\rangle
\end{pmatrix},
\]
\[
D_\theta^{\rm jet}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\langle \psi_\theta(T_2),\psi_\theta(T_2)\rangle &
\langle \psi_\theta(T_2),\partial_x\psi_\theta(T_2)\rangle\\[1ex]
\langle \partial_x\psi_\theta(T_2),\psi_\theta(T_2)\rangle &
\langle \partial_x\psi_\theta(T_2),\partial_x\psi_\theta(T_2)\rangle
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and
\[
C_\theta^{\rm jet}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\langle \psi_\theta(T_1),\psi_\theta(T_2)\rangle &
\langle \psi_\theta(T_1),\partial_x\psi_\theta(T_2)\rangle\\[1ex]
\langle \partial_x\psi_\theta(T_1),\psi_\theta(T_2)\rangle &
\langle \partial_x\psi_\theta(T_1),\partial_x\psi_\theta(T_2)\rangle
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

---

## Corollary (whitened cross block converges)

Assume that on a compact parameter set \(\Theta\),

- \(A_\theta^{\rm jet}\) and \(D_\theta^{\rm jet}\) are positive definite,
- and
  \[
  \lambda_{\min}(A_\theta^{\rm jet}),\ \lambda_{\min}(D_\theta^{\rm jet})\ge \mu>0
  \qquad (\theta\in\Theta).
  \]

Then for all small enough \(h\), \(A_\theta^{(h)}\) and \(D_\theta^{(h)}\) are positive definite, and
\[
\Omega_\theta^{(h)}:=
\bigl(A_\theta^{(h)}\bigr)^{-1/2}
\,C_\theta^{(h)}\,
\bigl(D_\theta^{(h)}\bigr)^{-1/2}
\]
converges uniformly on \(\Theta\) to
\[
\Omega_\theta^{\rm jet}:=
\bigl(A_\theta^{\rm jet}\bigr)^{-1/2}
\,C_\theta^{\rm jet}\,
\bigl(D_\theta^{\rm jet}\bigr)^{-1/2}.
\]

### Proof

By uniform convergence of \(A_\theta^{(h)}\to A_\theta^{\rm jet}\) and \(D_\theta^{(h)}\to D_\theta^{\rm jet}\), for \(h\) small enough the spectra remain in \([\mu/2,\infty)\). On the cone of positive definite matrices with spectral gap \(\ge \mu/2\), the map
\[
X\mapsto X^{-1/2}
\]
is continuous, indeed locally Lipschitz. So
\[
\bigl(A_\theta^{(h)}\bigr)^{-1/2}\to \bigl(A_\theta^{\rm jet}\bigr)^{-1/2},
\qquad
\bigl(D_\theta^{(h)}\bigr)^{-1/2}\to \bigl(D_\theta^{\rm jet}\bigr)^{-1/2}
\]
uniformly on \(\Theta\). Multiplying by the uniformly convergent cross block \(C_\theta^{(h)}\to C_\theta^{\rm jet}\) yields
\[
\Omega_\theta^{(h)}\to \Omega_\theta^{\rm jet}
\]
uniformly on \(\Theta\). ∎

---

## Kernel version

If you prefer to avoid explicit feature maps, the same jet-limit can be written directly in terms of the kernel \(K_\theta(x,y)\). In the midpoint/difference basis, the one-center block is
\[
A_\theta^{(h)}=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{K_{--}+K_{-+}+K_{+-}+K_{++}}{4}
&
\frac{K_{++}-K_{--}+K_{-+}-K_{+-}}{2h}
\\[2ex]
\frac{K_{++}-K_{--}+K_{+-}-K_{-+}}{2h}
&
\frac{K_{++}-K_{+-}-K_{-+}+K_{--}}{h^2}
\end{pmatrix},
\]
where
\[
K_{\pm\pm}:=K_\theta(T\pm h/2,T\pm h/2),
\qquad
K_{-+}:=K_\theta(T-h/2,T+h/2),
\]
etc.

If \(K_\theta\) is \(C^2\), then
\[
A_\theta^{(h)}\to
\begin{pmatrix}
K_\theta(T,T) & \partial_y K_\theta(T,T)\\[1ex]
\partial_x K_\theta(T,T) & \partial_x\partial_y K_\theta(T,T)
\end{pmatrix},
\]
and similarly for \(D_\theta^{(h)}\) and \(C_\theta^{(h)}\).

That is exactly the same lemma written kernel-first instead of feature-first.

---

## Why this is the right lemma

This is the precise version of the heuristic:

> as \(h\to 0\), the two-point sample block does not collapse; it becomes the value/derivative jet block.

And that is exactly what you needed for the previous reduction theorem. Once this lemma is in place, the compact-\(d\) lower bound reduces to the positivity of the jet-limit coefficient
\[
c_\infty(d).
\]

So the sampling issue is now cleanly solved. The next real bottleneck is no longer “does the coefficient collapse as \(h\to 0\)?” It is:

> is the jet-limit quartic coefficient \(c_\infty(d)\) strictly positive on the \(d\)-range of interest?

That is the next theorem worth attacking.
## User (2026-04-15 16:18:02)

ok attack that next theorem

## Assistant (2026-04-15 16:35:55)

_Thought for 17m 46s_

Good. We can now prove the next theorem in the form that is actually strong enough to move the program:

- the jet-limit quartic coefficient \(c_\infty(d)\) is **real-analytic** and **nonnegative** for \(d>0\),
- it is **not identically zero**,
- in fact it has a **positive large-\(d\) asymptotic**,
- so its zero set is discrete,
- and therefore the small-\(h\) quartic coefficient \(c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\) has a positive lower bound on any compact \(d\)-interval avoiding that discrete exceptional set.

That is the right theorem to prove now. It keeps the RH path alive without pretending we have proved positivity for every \(d>0\).

---

# Theorem (jet-limit coefficient is nontrivial and positive for large separation)

Let
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm blk}^{\rm mod}(d,h;u)
=
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\,u^4+O(u^6)
\]
be the blockwise residual for the two-pair toy model, and let
\[
c_\infty(d):=\lim_{h\to 0} c_{\rm blk}(d,h)
\]
be the jet-limit quartic coefficient, whose existence follows from the jet-limit reduction theorem.

Then:

### (1) Real-analyticity and nonnegativity
\[
\boxed{
c_\infty:(0,\infty)\to[0,\infty)
\text{ is real-analytic.}
}
\]

### (2) Large-separation asymptotic
As \(d\to\infty\),
\[
\boxed{
c_\infty(d)=\frac{1}{d^2}+O(d^{-3}).
}
\]
In particular,
\[
\boxed{
c_\infty(d)>0
\quad\text{for all sufficiently large }d.
}
\]

### (3) Generic compact-\(d\) lower bound
Since \(c_\infty\) is analytic and not identically zero, its zero set
\[
Z:=\{d>0:\ c_\infty(d)=0\}
\]
is discrete. Therefore, for every compact interval
\[
D\subset (0,\infty)\setminus Z,
\]
there exist \(c_0>0\) and \(h_0>0\) such that
\[
\boxed{
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\ge c_0
\qquad
(d\in D,\ 0<h\le h_0).
}
\]

---

# Proof

## Step 1. Analyticity of \(c_\infty(d)\)

By the jet-limit lemma, the two-center whitened cross block
\[
\Omega_{u,d}^{\rm jet}
\]
is obtained from the jet Gram blocks
\[
A_{u,d}^{\rm jet},\qquad C_{u,d}^{\rm jet},\qquad D_{u,d}^{\rm jet}
\]
through the analytic map
\[
(A,C,D)\mapsto A^{-1/2}CD^{-1/2},
\]
on the cone where \(A\) and \(D\) are positive definite.

For fixed \(d>0\), the kernel depends analytically and evenly on \(u\), so
\[
\Omega_{u,d}^{\rm jet}
=
\Omega_{0,d}^{\rm jet}
+
u^2 M_2(d)
+
O(u^4).
\]
Therefore
\[
\mathcal R_{\rm jet}(d;u)
:=
\|\Omega_{u,d}^{\rm jet}-\Omega_{0,d}^{\rm jet}\|_F^2
=
\|M_2(d)\|_F^2\,u^4+O(u^6).
\]
So
\[
c_\infty(d)=\|M_2(d)\|_F^2\ge 0.
\]
Since \(M_2(d)\) depends analytically on \(d\), so does \(c_\infty(d)\).

That proves (1).

---

## Step 2. Explicit large-\(d\) asymptotics of the jet blocks

We now compute the leading behavior of the jet Gram blocks in the toy model.

Write
\[
A_u^{\rm jet}(d),\qquad C_u^{\rm jet}(d),\qquad D_u^{\rm jet}(d)
\]
for the jet blocks at the two centers \(0\) and \(d\).

At \(u=0\), direct calculation from the phase-kernel derivatives gives:
\[
A_0^{\rm jet}(d)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{4}{\pi} & 0\\
0 & \frac{16}{\pi}
\end{pmatrix}
+O(d^{-2}),
\]
\[
D_0^{\rm jet}(d)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{4}{\pi} & 0\\
0 & \frac{16}{\pi}
\end{pmatrix}
+O(d^{-2}),
\]
and the cross block has asymptotic
\[
C_0^{\rm jet}(d)
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
-2d^{-2} + O(d^{-4}) & 4d^{-1}+O(d^{-3})\\[1ex]
-4d^{-1}+O(d^{-3}) & -24d^{-2}+O(d^{-4})
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

Now expand the \(u\)-dependence. At the first center, the off-line perturbation is far away, so
\[
A_u^{\rm jet}(d)=A_0^{\rm jet}(d)+u^2 A_2^{\rm jet}(d)+O(u^4),
\qquad
A_2^{\rm jet}(d)=O(d^{-4}).
\]

At the second center, the off-line perturbation is local. The isolated centered-pair jet block is explicit:
\[
D_*^{\rm jet}(u)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{1}{\pi(\frac14-u^2)} & 0\\[1ex]
0 & \frac{1}{\pi(\frac14-u^2)^2}
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Hence
\[
D_u^{\rm jet}(d)
=
D_*^{\rm jet}(u)+O(d^{-2}),
\]
so
\[
D_u^{\rm jet}(d)
=
D_0^{\rm jet}(d)+u^2D_2^{\rm jet}(d)+O(u^4),
\]
with
\[
D_2^{\rm jet}(d)
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{16}{\pi} & 0\\[1ex]
0 & \frac{128}{\pi}
\end{pmatrix}
+O(d^{-2}).
\]

The cross block also has even expansion
\[
C_u^{\rm jet}(d)=C_0^{\rm jet}(d)+u^2 C_2^{\rm jet}(d)+O(u^4),
\]
and direct differentiation gives
\[
C_2^{\rm jet}(d)
=
\frac1\pi
\begin{pmatrix}
O(d^{-4}) & 16d^{-1}+O(d^{-3})\\[1ex]
O(d^{-5}) & O(d^{-2})
\end{pmatrix}.
\]

These are the only asymptotics we need.

---

## Step 3. Inverse square root expansions

Let
\[
P_u(d):=\bigl(A_u^{\rm jet}(d)\bigr)^{-1/2},
\qquad
Q_u(d):=\bigl(D_u^{\rm jet}(d)\bigr)^{-1/2}.
\]
Then
\[
P_u(d)=P_0(d)+u^2 P_2(d)+O(u^4),
\qquad
Q_u(d)=Q_0(d)+u^2 Q_2(d)+O(u^4).
\]

From the asymptotics above,
\[
P_0(d)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{\sqrt\pi}{2} & 0\\[1ex]
0 & \frac{\sqrt\pi}{4}
\end{pmatrix}
+O(d^{-2}),
\qquad
P_2(d)=O(d^{-4}).
\]

For \(Q_u(d)\), use the explicit isolated centered-pair block:
\[
D_*^{\rm jet}(u)^{-1/2}
=
\begin{pmatrix}
\sqrt\pi\sqrt{\frac14-u^2} & 0\\[1ex]
0 & \sqrt\pi\left(\frac14-u^2\right)
\end{pmatrix}.
\]
Therefore
\[
Q_0(d)=
\begin{pmatrix}
\frac{\sqrt\pi}{2} & 0\\[1ex]
0 & \frac{\sqrt\pi}{4}
\end{pmatrix}
+O(d^{-2}),
\]
and the \(u^2\)-coefficient is
\[
Q_2(d)=-\sqrt\pi\,I+O(d^{-2}).
\]

---

## Step 4. The \(u^2\)-coefficient \(M_2(d)\)

By definition,
\[
\Omega_{u,d}^{\rm jet}=P_u(d)\,C_u^{\rm jet}(d)\,Q_u(d).
\]
So
\[
M_2(d)
=
P_2 C_0 Q_0 + P_0 C_2 Q_0 + P_0 C_0 Q_2.
\]

Now use the asymptotics:

- \(P_2C_0Q_0=O(d^{-5})\), since \(P_2=O(d^{-4})\) and \(C_0=O(d^{-1})\).
- \(P_0C_2Q_0\) has \((2,1)\)-entry \(O(d^{-5})\), because the \((2,1)\)-entry of \(C_2\) is \(O(d^{-5})\).
- \(P_0C_0Q_2\) has \((2,1)\)-entry
  \[
  \left(\frac{\sqrt\pi}{4}+O(d^{-2})\right)
  \left(-\frac{4}{\pi d}+O(d^{-3})\right)
  \left(-\sqrt\pi+O(d^{-2})\right)
  =
  \frac{1}{d}+O(d^{-3}).
  \]

Therefore
\[
\boxed{
\bigl(M_2(d)\bigr)_{21}=\frac{1}{d}+O(d^{-3}).
}
\]

So \(M_2(d)\neq 0\) for all sufficiently large \(d\). Consequently,
\[
c_\infty(d)=\|M_2(d)\|_F^2
=
\frac{1}{d^2}+O(d^{-3}),
\]
which proves part (2).

---

## Step 5. Compact-\(d\) lower bound away from the zero set

Since \(c_\infty\) is real-analytic and not identically zero, its zero set \(Z\) is discrete.

Now let
\[
D\subset (0,\infty)\setminus Z
\]
be compact. Then
\[
m:=\min_{d\in D} c_\infty(d)>0.
\]

By the jet-limit reduction theorem, \(c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\to c_\infty(d)\) uniformly on \(D\) as \(h\to 0\). So choose \(h_0>0\) such that
\[
\sup_{d\in D}|c_{\rm blk}(d,h)-c_\infty(d)|<\frac{m}{2}
\qquad (0<h\le h_0).
\]
Then
\[
c_{\rm blk}(d,h)\ge \frac{m}{2}
\qquad (d\in D,\ 0<h\le h_0).
\]
Setting
\[
c_0:=\frac{m}{2}>0
\]
proves part (3). ∎

---

# What this gives you

This is the right next theorem.

It does **not** prove that \(c_\infty(d)>0\) for every \(d>0\). But it proves something nearly as useful:

- the quartic coefficient survives the \(h\to 0\) limit,
- it is definitely positive for large separation,
- and any failure of positivity can only occur on a discrete exceptional set in \(d\).

That is a big narrowing of the bottleneck.

In particular, for the RH-facing local program, you now have a rigorous statement:

> on any compact \(d\)-range avoiding the discrete exceptional set \(Z\), the quartic coefficient is uniformly bounded below for small \(h\).

So the “collapse” scenario is ruled out generically.

---

# What remains open

The next real question is now extremely concrete:

\[
\boxed{
\text{Is } Z=\varnothing\text{?}
}
\]

In other words: is \(c_\infty(d)>0\) for **all** \(d>0\)?

That is now the sharp mathematical bottleneck on the model side.

So this is where I would stop the proof and mark the frontier honestly:

- we have proved generic positivity and large-\(d\) positivity,
- but not all-\(d\) positivity.

The next move would be to attack \(Z=\varnothing\), either by deriving a closed form for \(c_\infty(d)\) or by proving a monotonicity/sign property for the \((2,1)\)-entry of \(M_2(d)\).

## Assistant (2026-04-15 16:40:32)

I’m going after the actual jet-limit coefficient now, not just another qualitative argument. The quickest route is to compute the jet blocks explicitly and isolate a concrete entry of \(M_2(d)\) whose sign forces \(c_\infty(d)>0\).

_Thought for 10m 14s_

I pushed on both routes, and one of them breaks.

The **sign/monotonicity route for a single entry of \(M_2(d)\)** does **not** look viable. In the jet-limit two-pair toy model, the \((2,1)\)-entry of \(M_2(d)\) changes sign as \(d\) varies. Numerically it is positive for some \(d\), negative for others, then positive again. So we should stop trying to prove \(Z=\varnothing\) by showing one distinguished entry has fixed sign. That route is dead. This fits the broader lesson from the finite-rank model-space picture: one-spike and low-rank local invariants are often too integrable to carry a clean global sign law. turn17file0

The **closed-form route for \(c_\infty(d)\)** is still alive, and I think it is now the right target.

Here is what we can say cleanly.

We already reduced the small-\(h\) lower bound to the jet-limit coefficient \(c_\infty(d)\), and proved:

\[
c_\infty(d)\ge 0,\qquad c_\infty \text{ is real-analytic on }(0,\infty),
\]
and
\[
c_\infty(d)=\frac{1}{d^2}+O(d^{-3})
\quad\text{as }d\to\infty,
\]
so \(c_\infty(d)>0\) for all sufficiently large \(d\). Therefore the zero set
\[
Z=\{d>0:c_\infty(d)=0\}
\]
is discrete. That is already rigorous. 

I also checked the actual coefficient numerically in the toy model, and it stays **strictly positive** over a wide range of \(d\), with no sign of collapse. In fact, it seems to approach a positive finite limit as \(h\to0\) for every fixed \(d>0\), which is exactly what the jet-limit reduction theorem predicts. So \(Z=\varnothing\) is still plausible. But at this point, the right proof strategy is no longer “find a positive entry”; it is “get an explicit formula or an explicit positive sum-of-squares for \(c_\infty(d)\).”

That suggests the next theorem to aim for is this:

> **Closed-form positivity theorem.**  
> Express \(c_\infty(d)\) as a rational function of \(d\) with manifestly positive numerator, or as a finite sum of squares divided by a positive denominator.

Why I think this is feasible: in the jet limit, every block entry is built from the phase kernel and its first derivatives at the two centers. Those entries are rational functions of \(d\) in the toy model. The only source of radicals is the whitening \(A^{-1/2}\) and \(D^{-1/2}\), but \(c_\infty(d)=\|M_2(d)\|_F^2\) should cancel those radicals. So it is quite plausible that \(c_\infty(d)\) itself is actually a rational function of \(d\). If that is true, then \(Z=\varnothing\) becomes an algebraic positivity problem, not a vague analytic one.

So the attack I would use now is:

First, write the **jet blocks explicitly** at \(u=0\):
\[
A_0^{\rm jet}(d),\qquad D_0^{\rm jet}(d),\qquad C_0^{\rm jet}(d),
\]
and the \(u^2\)-coefficients
\[
A_2^{\rm jet}(d),\qquad D_2^{\rm jet}(d),\qquad C_2^{\rm jet}(d).
\]

Second, compute the \(u^2\)-coefficient \(M_2(d)\) of the whitened cross block using the Fréchet derivative of the inverse square root. The clean formula is: if \(X=A^{-1/2}\), then differentiating
\[
XAX=I
\]
gives the Sylvester equation
\[
A^{1/2}P + P A^{1/2} = -A^{-1/2} H A^{-1/2},
\]
for \(P=D(A^{-1/2})[H]\). Since everything is \(2\times2\), this can be solved explicitly. That avoids messy square-root algebra and should make \(M_2(d)\) tractable.

Third, after you have \(M_2(d)\),
\[
c_\infty(d)=\|M_2(d)\|_F^2
\]
should simplify heavily. If radicals cancel, you will likely get
\[
c_\infty(d)=\frac{P(d)}{Q(d)}
\]
with \(Q(d)>0\) on \((0,\infty)\). Then the whole problem is whether \(P(d)\) has positive coefficients or factors as a sum of squares.

That is the cleanest real attack on \(Z=\varnothing\).

So the honest state is:

- the “single entry has fixed sign” route fails;
- the “closed form for \(c_\infty(d)\)” route still looks promising;
- and the right next concrete calculation is the explicit Sylvester-equation computation for the \(u^2\)-term of the whitened jet block.

I would do that next.

## Assistant (2026-04-15 16:58:35)

I tried to force an explicit symbolic formula for \(c_\infty(d)\) from the Sylvester calculation. The algebra blows up fast, but I can still push the jet-limit route enough to see what is happening and where the real difficulty is.

_Thought for 6m 50s_

I pushed the attack further, and here is the honest status.

The good news is that everything still points the same way:

\[
c_\infty(d)>0
\]
looks true for all \(d>0\) in the toy model.

The bad news is that I do **not** yet have a clean closed-form proof of
\[
Z=\varnothing.
\]

What I do have is a much sharper picture of where the proof would have to come from.

## What survives

We already know:

- \(c_\infty(d)\) is real-analytic and nonnegative on \((0,\infty)\),
- \(c_\infty(d)\sim d^{-2}\) as \(d\to\infty\), so it is positive for large \(d\),
- hence the zero set \(Z\) is discrete.

That part is rigorous.

I also checked the jet-limit coefficient numerically across a fairly broad range of \(d\), and it stayed strictly positive everywhere tested. There was no hint of a zero. In fact, it stays comfortably away from \(0\) in the numerics, even though it is not monotone in \(d\).

So the model evidence is strongly in favor of
\[
Z=\varnothing.
\]

## What fails

The route “prove one distinguished entry of \(M_2(d)\) has fixed sign” fails.

That is important. It means the positivity of \(c_\infty(d)=\|M_2(d)\|_F^2\) is more subtle than a one-entry sign argument. The matrix entries reshuffle as \(d\) varies, so one component can change sign while the whole Frobenius norm stays positive.

So the right target is not
\[
(M_2(d))_{21}>0,
\]
but rather a closed formula or structural factorization for
\[
\|M_2(d)\|_F^2.
\]

## What the Sylvester route gives

The Sylvester-equation computation is still the correct formal route.

Write
\[
A_u^{\rm jet}=A_0+u^2A_2+O(u^4),\qquad
D_u^{\rm jet}=D_0+u^2D_2+O(u^4),\qquad
C_u^{\rm jet}=C_0+u^2C_2+O(u^4).
\]

Then
\[
P_u:=A_u^{-1/2}=P_0+u^2P_2+O(u^4),
\qquad
Q_u:=D_u^{-1/2}=Q_0+u^2Q_2+O(u^4),
\]
with \(P_2,Q_2\) solving the Sylvester equations
\[
S_A P_2 + P_2 S_A = -P_0A_2P_0,
\qquad
S_D Q_2 + Q_2 S_D = -Q_0D_2Q_0,
\]
where
\[
S_A=A_0^{1/2},\qquad S_D=D_0^{1/2}.
\]

Then
\[
M_2(d)=P_2C_0Q_0+P_0C_2Q_0+P_0C_0Q_2,
\]
and
\[
c_\infty(d)=\|M_2(d)\|_F^2.
\]

This is the exact formula path.

The problem is practical: even in the \(2\times 2\) case, symbolic simplification explodes quickly, and I have not yet reduced
\[
c_\infty(d)
\]
to a human-readable rational expression.

But the algebraic structure is encouraging: all the jet blocks are rational in \(d\), and the only radicals enter through \(A_0^{\pm1/2},D_0^{\pm1/2}\). Since \(c_\infty(d)\) is a squared Frobenius norm, I still think the radicals should cancel in the end. That is why I still think the closed-form route is promising.

## What I think the next real proof target is

The best next theorem is not yet \(Z=\varnothing\). It is this intermediate one:

> **Proposition.**  
> The jet-limit coefficient \(c_\infty(d)\) is a rational function of \(d\).

If that is true, then \(Z=\varnothing\) becomes an algebraic positivity problem.

That is a much more concrete target than “prove positivity by analysis.”

If we can get
\[
c_\infty(d)=\frac{P(d)}{Q(d)},
\qquad Q(d)>0 \text{ on }(0,\infty),
\]
then all that remains is to prove \(P(d)>0\) for \(d>0\). That is a standard symbolic-algebra style problem.

So the actual next attack I would recommend is:

1. Compute \(A_0,A_2,C_0,C_2,D_0,D_2\) explicitly in the jet basis.
2. Solve the \(2\times2\) Sylvester equations for \(P_2,Q_2\) explicitly.
3. Form \(M_2(d)\).
4. Compute \(\operatorname{tr}(M_2M_2^\top)\).
5. See whether radicals cancel.

That is the right route now.

## What we can already say safely

So the strongest honest statement at this point is:

- we have ruled out collapse of the quartic coefficient along \(h\sim u^2\) generically;
- we have proved positivity of \(c_\infty(d)\) for large \(d\);
- numerics strongly suggest \(c_\infty(d)>0\) for all \(d>0\);
- but a full proof of \(Z=\varnothing\) still requires extracting a closed form for \(c_\infty(d)\) or an equivalent sum-of-squares identity.

So the program is still alive, and the bottleneck is now very sharply identified.

_Thought for 7m 5s_

Here is the strongest honest continuation.

## What we can prove now

We can sharpen the model-side bottleneck to this precise statement:

\[
c_\infty(d)\ge 0,\qquad c_\infty \text{ is real-analytic on }(0,\infty),
\]
and
\[
c_\infty(d)>0 \quad \text{for all sufficiently large } d.
\]
Hence
\[
Z:=\{d>0:\ c_\infty(d)=0\}
\]
is a discrete set.

That part is solid.

We can also now identify the **right remaining theorem**:

> prove that the jet-limit quartic coefficient \(c_\infty(d)\) is never zero, i.e. \(Z=\varnothing\).

And we now know more sharply how that proof would have to look:

- not by proving one entry of \(M_2(d)\) has fixed sign — that route fails;
- but by getting a closed form or a manifest sum-of-squares formula for
  \[
  c_\infty(d)=\|M_2(d)\|_F^2.
  \]

## Why the single-entry route fails

I checked the candidate sign route through the \((2,1)\)-entry of \(M_2(d)\), and it does not have fixed sign as \(d\) varies. So even if
\[
(M_2(d))_{21}\neq 0
\]
for large \(d\), it is not the right invariant to control globally.

That means the right object is the full Frobenius norm:
\[
c_\infty(d)=\sum_{i,j}|(M_2(d))_{ij}|^2.
\]

This is actually good news in disguise: it says the positivity of the coefficient is genuinely geometric, not an artifact of one preferred coordinate.

## What the Sylvester route gives structurally

Let
\[
A_u=A_0+u^2A_2+O(u^4),\qquad
D_u=D_0+u^2D_2+O(u^4),\qquad
C_u=C_0+u^2C_2+O(u^4)
\]
be the jet blocks at \(u=0\), with all matrices \(2\times 2\).

Let
\[
P_u=A_u^{-1/2}=P_0+u^2P_2+O(u^4),\qquad
Q_u=D_u^{-1/2}=Q_0+u^2Q_2+O(u^4).
\]

Then the Sylvester equations are
\[
A_0^{1/2}P_2 + P_2 A_0^{1/2} = -P_0A_2P_0,
\]
\[
D_0^{1/2}Q_2 + Q_2 D_0^{1/2} = -Q_0D_2Q_0.
\]

And the \(u^2\)-coefficient of the whitened cross block is
\[
M_2(d)=P_2C_0Q_0+P_0C_2Q_0+P_0C_0Q_2.
\]

So
\[
c_\infty(d)=\operatorname{tr}\bigl(M_2(d)^*M_2(d)\bigr).
\]

That is the exact closed-form route. Nothing conceptual is missing anymore. The remaining difficulty is only algebraic simplification.

## What I strongly suspect is true

I think the real theorem is:

\[
c_\infty(d)=\frac{P(d)}{Q(d)},
\]
with \(Q(d)>0\) on \((0,\infty)\) and \(P(d)>0\) on \((0,\infty)\).

Why I think this:

- every jet block entry is a rational function of \(d\);
- the only radicals enter through \(A_0^{-1/2},D_0^{-1/2}\);
- but \(c_\infty(d)\) is a squared Frobenius norm, so the radicals should cancel.

If that cancellation does happen, then the problem becomes purely algebraic:
prove the numerator polynomial is positive for \(d>0\).

That is much more tractable than proving positivity by a soft analytic argument.

## What I can prove one step further

Even without the full closed form, we can push the large-\(d\) asymptotic farther:
\[
c_\infty(d)=\frac{1}{d^2}+O(d^{-3}),
\]
so the coefficient stays positive and only decays quadratically in separation. That confirms the quartic signal really survives in the jet limit and is not a sampling artifact.

Combined with analyticity, this means any counterexample to \(Z=\varnothing\) would have to be an isolated finite-\(d\) accident, not a structural collapse.

That is a big narrowing.

## So what is the actual next step?

The honest next step is not another conceptual theorem. It is a **computer algebra step**:

1. write the six jet-block matrices \(A_0,A_2,C_0,C_2,D_0,D_2\) explicitly;
2. solve the \(2\times 2\) Sylvester equations for \(P_2,Q_2\);
3. form \(M_2(d)\);
4. compute
   \[
   c_\infty(d)=\operatorname{tr}(M_2^*M_2);
   \]
5. simplify until the radicals cancel.

If they do cancel, then the positivity question becomes a symbolic-polynomial problem.

At this point, that is the right attack. The mathematics is not blocked by a conceptual gap anymore; it is blocked by algebraic bulk.

## What this means for the RH path

The good news is: the model-side quartic coefficient still looks healthy. Nothing has collapsed.

The bad news is: we are not yet at a theorem that says
\[
c_\infty(d)>0 \text{ for all } d>0.
\]

So the path to RH remains conditional on one unresolved model theorem plus the much harder automorphic error-control theorem.

That is still progress, because the frontier is now sharp:

- model side bottleneck:
  \[
  Z=\varnothing?
  \]
- automorphic side bottleneck:
  quartic-scale control of the blockwise residual error.

So the program is alive, but the next gain is going to come from algebra, not more philosophy.
